Exploring Machine Learning Methods for Forecasting IoT Measurements in Wooden Structures

==PAGE:5==
Abstract
The construction industry accounts for a large proportion of global greenhouse gas emissions.
Building in wood is viewed as a viable solution to reduce emissions. However, the possibility of too
high moisture content in wood can lead to mold growth that needs to be avoided during and after
the construction process. Woodsense ApS offers IoT sensors that can monitor moisture content
in construction wood. Forecasts of the moisture content per sensor can enable proactive planning
such that tooôÙhigh moisture contents can be avoided during and after the construction process.
This thesis focuses on creating 14ôÙday probabilistic moisture content forecasts of each sensor of
Woodsense by utilizing sensor data and weather forecasts. Additionally, the thesis focuses on
providing explanations of the forecasts to strengthen decisionôÙmaking during planning.
The forecasting performance and explainability correctness of the machine learning models LightôÙ
GBM and Temporal Fusion Transformer are explored in two experiments with baseline models to
identify the model most suitable for providing explainable moisture content forecasts. A synthetic
dataset is used for comparing explainability correctness, and a public PM2.5 dataset is used to
compare forecasting performance. Vector Autoregression and SARIMAX models are respectively
used as a baseline for the explainability and forecasting experiments. A variant of the Temporal
Fusion Transformer without skip connections passing the attention layer is found to have overall
best forecasting and explainability capabilities.
The Temporal Fusion Transformer is trained on sensor data and historic weather data of WoodôÙ
sense. It is feasible to use the Temporal Fusion Transformer without skip connections to provide
probabilistic explainable moisture content forecasts that can give useful information in proactive
decisionôÙmaking and planning. However, wrong weather forecasts can lead to significantly wrong
forecasts. Also, more work needs to be investigated regarding making the provided explainability
easier and faster to comprehend.
Exploring Machine Learning Methods for Forecasting IoT Measurements in Wooden Structures

1
Introduction
In 2020, the construction industry accounted for 37% of global greenhouse gas emissions, where
10% of emissions were from the manufacturing of construction materials [1]. Building in wood has
been viewed as a viable solution to reduce carbon emissions [2]. However, lack of knowledge in
building in wood and avoiding moisture damages, such as mold growth or rot, are barriers that
keep the industry from building in wood [3]. Mold growth and rot are related to wood moisture
content, where it is advised that the moisture content should be kept below 16% to avoid the risk
of mold growth [4]. The mold growth risk depends on the amount of time the wood has been
exposed to too high moisture levels or to too high humidity levels. Depending on the temperature
and moisture, the mold can develop from 0ôÙ12 weeks [5].
The current modus operandi is to manually measure moisture content by using an insertion moisôÙ
ture meter which is costly in time and personôÙhours. Woodsense ApS eases the monitoring proôÙ
cess and alleviates the fear of moisture damage in the construction industry by offering IoT sensors
that monitor moisture content and surrounding humidity and temperature. Being able to monitor
the moisture of the wood is useful for the customers both during and after the construction proôÙ
cess. During the construction process, it is advised that the moisture content of the wood must not
exceed 16% when enclosed (e.g., by walls) to avoid the risk of mold [4], which can be challenging
to plan after. After construction, the sensors make it possible to monitor the moisture content even
though the wood is not accessible.
The main focus of this thesis is to explore the feasibility of applying stateôÙofôÙtheôÙart dataôÙdriven
models to forecast moisture content in wood used in construction. Specifically, the objective is
to provide useful probabilistic forecasts of moisture content 14 days into the future by utilizing
sensor data and weather forecasts. Additionally, it is explored if the explainability of the forecasts
can provide helpful information about the environment of the sensor. By providing the forecasts
with prediction intervals and informative explanations, the customerãs trust in the model can be
strengthened [6] and decisionôÙmaking capabilities can be empowered. Woodsense ApS supplies
the dataset consisting of sensor data and historic and forecasted weather data.
1.1
Typical Use Cases
Typical use cases are provided to understand how the customer can utilize explainable forecasts.
The use cases are listed below:
1. The customer wants to know if they can enclose the wooden structure of the building in the
coming week. However, it will be raining for the next two days. The forecast shows the moisôÙ
ture content increases above 16% during rainfall, but it also shows that the moisture content
decreases below 16% again in the following week. The model shows rain is an important
factor during an increase in moisture, and humidity and temperature are important factors
during a decrease in moisture. This makes the customer trust the forecast and reschedule
to enclosing the wood in two weeks.
2. Forecasts have shown to be significantly wrong for an enclosed sensor, indicating something
has changed in the environment. The user gets an alarm for this change.
3. A customer can see that the moisture content of a sensor placed below a roof increases.
Forecasts inform the customer that values are expected to increase to levels associated with
the risk of mold in the next two weeks. The customer proactively checks the construction
the following day.
4. The same customer in case 3 would ideally like to know from the forecast explanations if
the increase is related to natural changes in the season or if it is due to water leakage. The
feature importance of the forecast reveals that rain is an important feature that had not been
important in prior forecasts, indicating to the customer that there has been a leak. Ideally, the
model will also capture when the modelãs temporal dynamics change so that the customer
can infer when the leak started supporting the customer in their troubleshooting.
Exploring Machine Learning Methods for Forecasting IoT Measurements in Wooden Structures
1
==PAGE:11==
Use cases 1ôÙ3 are the primary use cases of the thesis, while use case 4 is more ideal and secôÙ
ondary in the scope of the thesis.
1.2
Current Methods for Estimating Moisture Content and
Risk of Mold Growth in Construction Wood
To the extent of the authorãs knowledge, no forecasting methods or tools are developed to specifôÙ
ically forecast moisture content in construction wood. The closest found methods is by estimating
the moisture content or risk of mold based on temperature and relative humidity, assuming both
are constant in infinite time. To understand how moisture content is estimated, it will be described
how temperature and humidity affect the moisture content.
The moisture content of wood is measured in the weight percentage of the amount of water in
the wood with respect to the dry wood weight. The moisture content is highly dependent on the
characteristics of the environment since wood is an organic material that absorbs or desorbs waôÙ
ter from/to the surrounding air. The moisture content can be approximated with respect to relative
humidity but it depends on whether the wood is absorbing or desorbing water [7] as seen in Figure
1.1. Air humidity is often measured in the metric relative humidity, the percentage of the amount
of moisture that can be retained by the atmosphere. The relative humidity is dependent on temôÙ
perature as an increase in temperature will result in a decrease in relative humidity, assuming a
constant level of moisture in the air.
Figure 1.1: Absorption and desorption curve for moisture content in wood. The figure is from
ãFugtmûËling i trûÎ og tagkonstruktionerã [7].
The moisture content is a delayed response to the humidity and temperature levels in the air. The
speed of change in moisture content is dependent on many factors, such as if the wood is absorbôÙ
ing or desorbing water, the properties of the wood, and whether the air is stagnant or moving. No
formula considers all these factors where the closest is the ãEquilibrium Moisture Contentã formula
(EMC) [8] which is shown in Equation 1.1. Here, T is the temperature in degrees Celsius (ô¯C) and
H is relative humidity (%). The formula assumes constant humidity and temperature conditions in
infinite time and does not consider the type of wood, absorption/desorption, or ventilation factors.
EMC = 1800

Given the challenge to estimate moisture content, the parameters of relative humidity and temôÙ
perature are often used to estimate the time to and risk of mold growth. Risk of mold starts at

==PAGE:12==
levels above 75% relative humidity as can be seen in Figure 1.2 where 75% relative humidity is
equivalent to 15ôÙ18% moisture content according to Figure 1.1. Figure 1.2 emphasizes that 14ôÙ
day forecasts are relevant in helping customers of Woodsense to react in time because mold can
grow in less than two weeks.
Figure 1.2: Risk of development of mold based on current relative humidity and temperature. Each
curve denotes the minimum number of days (d) for mold to grow. The figure is from ãVejledning
om hûËndtering af fugt i byggerietã [4].
Both methods assume constant humidity and temperature, which rarely is the case, and neglect
other important factors such as absorption and desorption.
There also exists some work in developing models to forecast moisture content in drying chambers
which will be described in Section 1.3.4.
1.3
Related Work
To the extent of the authorãs knowledge, there does not exist any prior literature that is directly
comparable to forecasting moisture content in construction wood. Therefore, this section will reôÙ
view stateôÙofôÙtheôÙart dataôÙdriven forecasting methods relevant to forecasting moisture content.
The dataôÙdriven approach is chosen since it is hypothesized that a dataôÙdriven model will be able
to learn all the factors affecting moisture content and adopt forecasts to the sensorãs environment
and current behavior (e.g., is the wood absorbing or desorbing water).
The first half of the section will focus on stateôÙofôÙtheôÙart methods and models within dataôÙdriven
forecasting. The second half will focus on the stateôÙofôÙtheôÙart methods within explainable AI. FiôÙ
nally, stateôÙofôÙtheôÙart models within multiôÙhorizon probabilistic forecasting and explainability are
described.
1.3.1
Current StateôÙofôÙtheôÙart Methods in DataôÙdriven Forecasting
There is no consensus in the literature on whether statistical or machine learning methods are
stateôÙofôÙtheôÙart.
In literature, statistical methods refer to classic forecasting methods such as
SARIMAX [9] and Vector Autoregression (VAR) [10]. In literature, the main differences are idenôÙ
tified to be that statistical methods have structural assumptions such as linearity, fixed temporal
dependencies, and assumptions of stationary. In contrast, machine learning methods learn the
interôÙdependencies from the data [11, 12]. This means that statistical methods often acquire more
domain knowledge than machine learning methods [13]. However, AutoôÙARIMA has been deôÙ
veloped to ease the fitting of ARIMA models by automatically determining the order of the autoôÙ
regressive and movingôÙaverage components [14]. This makes ARIMA easier to use in operational
tasks where there is multiple time series. The validity of the distinctions between statistical and
machine learning methods is discussed in the research community [15] but will be used in this
thesis for clarity.
Another distinction is that statistical methods are local learners while machine learnings are global
learners [15]. Given S time series of the monthly sale of beers in S stores, the local method would
Exploring Machine Learning Methods for Forecasting IoT Measurements in Wooden Structures
3
==PAGE:13==
train S models to forecast beer for each store separately. The global method trains one model to
forecast for all S stores.
Statistical methods have long been referred to as stateôÙofôÙtheôÙart within forecasting, where the
performance of machine learning methods has long been questioned by literature [16, 17, 18].
The opinion in the research community that statistical methods are better suited for forecasting
was mainly based on forecasting competitions such as the M3 competition in 2000 [19], NN3
competition in 2006 [20], and NN5 in competition 2008 [21] as well other extensive comparisons
of forecasting methods [13, 22]. In these competitions, machine learning methods generally perôÙ
formed poorer than statistical methods. The forecasting competitions are open to all, and work by
a dataset is shared with the attendees, who are free to solve the problem as they seem fit. The
machine learning methods included in the competitions are among other Support Vector RegresôÙ
sion (SVR) [23], K Nearest Neighbor [24], Gaussian Processes [25], Recurrent Neural Network
(RNN) [26] and Long ShortôÙTerm Memory cell (LSTM) [27].
The main reasons identified for the inferiority of machine learning methods are the flexibility of
machine learning models can result in overfitting in low data regimes and the modelsã sensitivôÙ
ity to preprocessing of inputs and sparse events [16, 28, 29]. After the M4 competition in 2018
[30], the view on machine learning methods started to change partly because the winner was a
hybrid statistical and deep learning method called ESôÙRNN [31] and because a machine learning
method that utilized global learning (referred to as crossôÙlearning in paper) performed comparably
to statistical methods. After the M4 competition, a pure deep learning model called NôÙBEATS [32]
outperformed the ESôÙRNN by 3% on the M4 dataset.
Recent literature argue that machine learning methods are stateôÙofôÙtheôÙart within forecasting of
multivariate highôÙvolume data with multiple time series where they can benefit from global learning
[9, 15, 33]. The M3 and M4 competitions consist of time series data sets with different lengths
and intervals with no described similarities, which makes it challenging to train machine learning
methods globally. This is probably why machine learning methods perform inferior in competitions
that are suitable for local learning. To meet the demand for a dataset fitting for global learning, the
M5 competition was held [34, 35]. The dataset consisted of sales data from WallôÙMart, which is
much more suitable for global learning. In the competition, the machine learning method LightGBM
[36] dominated while other deep learning methods such as NôÙBEATS [26] and DeepAR [29] were
also among the top performers. LightGBM is a gradientôÙboosted model based on decision trees
developed by Microsoft in 2017. The performance of LightGBM has led to increased interest in
using treeôÙbased forecasting [37].
Concerning computational complexity, statistical methods have been argued to be the preferred
method as opposed to particularly deep learning methods. However, even though deep learning
methods have higher training time than statistical methods, they need less continuous training,
while statistical methods are updated continuously on each time series [15]. On the other hand,
deep learning methods have many hyperôÙparameters it is sensitive to, which can lead to the need
for many training sessions that are computationally costly [17].
Except for XGBoost [38], and LightGBM [36], it is found that most deep learning models are argued
to be among stateôÙofôÙtheôÙart which probably is due to the success of deep learning within Natural
Language Processing (NLP) and Computer Vision tasks. The rest of the this section will focus
on machine learning methods as they probably are more suitable to the Woodsense data that
consists of multiple multivariate time series from different sensors.
Deep learning architectures using LSTM cells, Convolutional Neural Networks (CNN) and AttenôÙ
tion will be described throughout the section. If the reader needs background knowledge of the
applied architectures, the architectures are shortly described in Appendix A.1.
1.3.2
MultiôÙhorizon Forecasting Methods
There are two main methods in the literature for creating multiôÙhorizon forecasting: the iterative
and direct method [16, 39]. The iterative method (also known as the recursive method) recursively
uses the former predicted target(s) as input to the next forecast as shown in (a) in Figure 1.3. The
direct method directly predicts multiôÙforecasting horizons. In literature, the direct methods refers
to creating forecasts by either creating K forecasting outputs with K models [39] or by sequenceôÙ
4
Exploring Machine Learning Methods for Forecasting IoT Measurements in Wooden Structures
==PAGE:14==
2ôÙsequence modeling [16]. The sequenceôÙ2ôÙsequence method is used for Deep Learning models
where the model encodes input known until inference time. The encoder output is passed to the
decoder that creates multiôÙhorizon forecasts as shown in illustration (b) in Figure 1.3. The decoder
can take future known observations as input, contrary to the iterative method that assumes all inôÙ
puts are known at inference time. The moisture content, humidity and temperature data measured
by the sensors of Woodsense is not known at inference time, which makes the direct method more
suitable.
Figure 1.3: Illustration of iterative and direct forecasting. (a) The iterative method recursively
reuses the predictions. (b) The direct method (here shown with sequence2sequence) creates
forecasts by encoding known input until inference time that is passed to a decoder that creates
predictions and can take future known variables as input. The figure is from ãTime Series ForeôÙ
casting with Deep Learning: A Surveyã [16].
It is argued that the direct method is more suitable for multiôÙhorizon forecasts. That is because
of error accumulation in the iterative process since the iterative approach reuses the predicted
targets [33, 40, 41, 42]. However, if the model is correctly specified, the iterative method will result
in better results because it will benefit from more training examples to tune the parameters [43].
1.3.3
Probabilistic Forecasting Methods
It has been implied in the literature that statistical methods provide probabilistic forecasts, and
machine learning methods provide points forecasts [15, 22]. Statistical methods offer probabilistic
forecasts approximated from residuals in training and parametric assumptions [44]. However, the
amount of research in probabilistic forecasting in machine learning has been growing in recent
years [15]. This section will focus on the current research on providing probabilistic forecasts in
machine learning.
There are basically three methods in training machine learning models to provide probabilistic
forecasts which are 1) by assuming a probability distribution, or 2) quantile regression [45], or 3)
by using a hybrid deep learning model, for example, by parameterization of a stateôÙspace model
by a deep neural network [9]. Method one and two are also referred to as parametric and nonôÙ
parametric methods, respectively.
The parametric method learns the network to estimate the parameters of the distribution by using
the logôÙlikelihood as a loss function. The nonôÙparametric method does not make any assumptions
about the distribution of the residuals and estimates the quantiles by minimizing the quantile loss
(i.e., pinball loss) for the chosen quantiles [41]. This means that quantiles that are not included in
training cannot be inferred afterward. However, quantile loss usually gives better and more robust
forecasts than the parametric approach due to the nonôÙparametric assumptions [41]. On the other
hand, quantile regression can suffer from quantile crossing [46].
Monte Carlo dropout can also achieve probabilistic forecasts for deep learning models by producôÙ
ing an ensemble of forecasts using dropout during inference from which prediction intervals can
be calculated [47]. Prediction intervals can also be estimated by bootstrapping where multiple
models are trained on different bootstrapped samples [48]. However, this method is infeasible if
it takes hours or days to train the model.
It is preferred that the models learn the uncertainty of the forecasts themselves because it is
believed they can learn to adjust the prediction intervals accordingly to the specific forecasts. This
is useful for the sensor data of Woodsense where the sensors behave differently.
Exploring Machine Learning Methods for Forecasting IoT Measurements in Wooden Structures
5
==PAGE:15==
1.3.4
Current Approaches in Similar Domains
As mentioned, there is no literature about forecasting sensor values in construction wood. HowôÙ
ever, there is some literature on forecasting wood moisture in drying chambers. Forecasting of
air quality is another domain that is arguably similar. The PM2.5 content is dependent on factors
such as temperature and humidity, which moisture content also is dependent on.
Wood Drying
Forecasting moisture content and drying times in drying chambers can improve the quality of
timber production. The forecasting is done both by theoretical equations and simulations [49, 50,
51] and by using dataôÙdriven approaches where SVR are argued to be better due to the lowôÙdata
regime [52, 53]. Since these approaches are limited to drying chambers in closed, controlled
environments with few observations, they are not directly applicable to the forecasting problem
with the Woodsense sensor data.
Air Quality
To preserve human health, it is demanded that EU members inform their citizens when particulate
matter exceeds (or is expected to exceed) the threshold of 50 ôçg/m3 [9]. Methods to forecast
PM2.5 can be divided into two categories: simulationôÙbased and dataôÙdriven [9]. LOTUSôÙEUROS
is a simulation model for forecasting PM2.5 values. It is shown that the model has biased forecasts
due to unmodeled unknown factors [54].
Gradient boosted models including XGBoost and LightGBM are tested on air quality datasets
where LightGBM obtains lowest root mean square error (RMSE) [55, 56, 57]. Deep learning
models are also tested with success on the publicly available PM2.5 dataset from UCI [11, 58,
59]. A sequenceôÙ2ôÙsequence model with attention applied to the temporal and feature dimension
obtains good results and outperforms AR and LSTM in RMSE [58]. Another sequenceôÙ2ôÙsequence
model with LSTM in encoder and decoder and temporal attention outperforms ARIMA, VARMA,
and SVR in RMSE and mean absolute error (MAE) [59].
1.3.5
Explainable AI Methods
Research in explainable AI is an active research area with a significant body of literature. ThereôÙ
fore, this explanation will only be a superficial introduction to the whole field of explainability methôÙ
ods. Most literature on explainability within time series is based on time series classification tasks.
Due to statistical methods being viewed as being interpretable due to the weights of the model
can be interpreted, the section focuses on explainability methods for machine learning methods.
There are two main methods for obtaining explainable forecasts: postôÙhoc and anteôÙhoc. AnteôÙ
hoc methods have buildôÙin interpretability ready from training making it modelôÙspecific [6, 16, 60].
PostôÙhoc methods are developed to interpret deep learning networks by extracting relationships
between feature input and predictions. PostôÙhoc models can both be modelôÙagnostic, meaning
they can explain any model or modelôÙspecific, meaning they can explain specific types of models
or networks. PostôÙhoc methods used for deep learning are also referred to as saliency methôÙ
ods. Furthermore, explanation methods can give local or global explanations. Local methods are
sampleôÙspecific, while global methods explain a subset of the dataset or the whole dataset [6].
Some explainability methods can provide both local and global explanations.
AnteôÙhoc methods in deep learning are mainly based on attention layers that apply variable and
temporalôÙwise attention on the features. This means that the attention weights can give local interôÙ
pretability [16]. Popular postôÙhoc methods are Local Interpretable ModelôÙAgnostic Explanations
(LIME) [61] and Shapley Additive Explanations (SHAP) [62] which are perturbation methods that
make perturbation to the input to estimate the importance of the features influence on the final preôÙ
diction. SHAP has become a popular explainability framework that can provide local and global
explainability based on Shapley values. Shapley values originate from game theory and estimate
a playerãs contribution (in this case, a feature) to the overall outcome of the game (forecast) by
evaluating each combination of players. Due to it being costly to estimate all feature combinations
as it would require training many models, SHAP estimates the feature importance by mimicking a
features is missing through perturbations to the input.
XGBoost with SHAP has been applied to model and explain accidents where factors impacting
the probability of an accident such as speed and weather conditions were inferred [63]. Similarly,
6
Exploring Machine Learning Methods for Forecasting IoT Measurements in Wooden Structures
==PAGE:16==
LightGBM has been applied with SHAP to infer and quantify the effects of risk factors in accidents
[64].
Other postôÙhoc explanation methods developed for deep learning are provided in Appendix A.2.
Additionally, Appendix A.3 provides a review of discussion in the literature on whether postôÙhoc or
anteôÙhoc are the best explainability method.
1.3.6
Empirical Testing of Explainable AI
Most evaluation methods are based on expert assessments and time series classification [6]. It
was only possible to find a single paper related to forecasting which evaluates the explainability
of attentionôÙbased modelsã ability to provide correct explainability concerning dependence on variôÙ
ables and lags on ten synthetic multivariate autoregressive multivariate datasets with increasing
complexity [65]. IMVôÙLSTM [66] is the best model to infer correct dependence and lags. The
model is explained in more detail in the following section.
A more comprehensive description of evaluations of explainable AI can be found in Appendix A.4.
1.3.7
StateôÙofôÙtheôÙart Forecasting Models for Multivariate HighôÙvolume
Data
The following section will describe models argued by researchers behind the model to be among
the stateôÙofôÙtheôÙart with respect to multiôÙhorizon forecasting or explainability on multivariate highôÙ
volume data. All the listed models were considered to be used for moisture content forecasting.
Many models benchmark forecasting performance on the publicly available electric1 and traffic2
datasets which makes the models comparable but also means that the argument of stateôÙofôÙtheôÙart
forecasting performance is limited to the specific datasets.
DeepAR is an RNNôÙbased model that creates multiôÙhorizon forecasts iteratively developed by
Amazon [29]. The model is often compared to other deep learning models. The probabilistic foreôÙ
casts are done by the parametric method. The model makes multiple sample trajectories during
inference by sampling from the parametric distribution in each time step to create probabilistic
forecasts.
MQôÙRNN/CNN is an encoderôÙdecoder framework that, with an RNN or CNN, encodes temporal
information and decodes with multiôÙlayer perceptions, which means it uses the direct forecasting
method. Probabilistic forecasts are estimated with the nonôÙparametric approach [41]. DeepTCN
is another model [40] that is very similar to MQôÙCNN, except that MQôÙCNN makes both static and
temporal encodings used in the decoder. The DeepTCN outperforms LightGBM and SARIMA
on a demand forecasting dataset and DeepAR, DeepState and AutoôÙArima on electric and traffic
datasets. No comparable results are provided from MQôÙRNN/CNN paper [41].
Temporal Fusion Transformer (TFT) is an encoderôÙdecoder based architecture that creates direct
forecasts with nonôÙparametric probabilistic forecasts [42]. TFT uses a variable selection network
with a softmax function to weigh the importance of the different features. The output is fed into an
LSTM. The output of the LSTM at each time step is fed into a multiôÙhead attention layer where the
output from each query and key matrices are accumulated before being multiplied with the value
matrix to make it more interpretable. Thus, multiôÙhead attention provides temporal importance.
The TFT is specifically designed to use covariate static data by using encoders that create context
embeddings to condition temporal dynamics by conditioning the variable selection network and
LSTM layers. The TFT outperforms DeepAR, DeepState ConvTrans and MQôÙRNN on 24 hour
horizon forecasts on the electric and traffic datasets. The variable selection network and attention
layers make the TFT an explainable anteôÙhoc model.
LightGBM, as mentioned earlier, outperforms DeepAR and NôÙBEATS in the M5 competition. The
LightGBM python implementation by Microsoft has builtôÙin SHAP and quantile regression impleôÙ
mentation. The implementation successfully provides quantile forecasts of NO2 levels where it
performs better than Quantile Linear Regression, Random Forest, KôÙNearest Neighbors, and MulôÙ
tilayer Perceptrons [67].
1https://archive.ics.uci.edu/ml/datasets/ElectricityLoadDiagrams20112014
2https://archive.ics.uci.edu/ml/datasets/PEMSôÙSF
Exploring Machine Learning Methods for Forecasting IoT Measurements in Wooden Structures
7
==PAGE:17==
Among explainable anteôÙhoc models, IMVôÙLSTM was considered [66]. The model has the hidden
state vector of an LSTM changed to a matrix to learn variableôÙwise hidden states in each row.
To understand the role of each variable in the prediction, they use variableôÙwise attention and
temporal attention on each variable hidden state in the LSTM. IMVôÙLSTM creates point forecasts
with the iterative method. The model also obtains the best explainability in the paper mentioned
in Section 1.3.6.
Other found models that briefly were considered concerning forecasting and explainability can
be found in Appendix A.5 where among others, ConvTrans and DeepState (to which TFT was
compared) are explained.
1.4
Motivation for Choice of Models
This section will explain the motivation for the choice of the baseline and main models. The modôÙ
els are chosen concerning forecasting and explainability capabilities. Even though they are not
explainable, models such as DeepAR and DeepTCN were considered since postôÙhoc methods
can make them explainable.
The variants of ARIMA (VAR and SARIMAX) are chosen as a baseline model because they are well
understood and explainable. They are also still considered stateôÙofôÙtheôÙart on lowôÙvolume data.
SVR was also considered since it is used in modeling moisture content in drying chambers [52,
53]. It was not chosen because it was inferior in forecasting performance to ARIMA in comparative
studies [13, 22].
LightGBM is chosen because it was a winning model in the M5 competition [34, 35]. The SHAP
approach is chosen to provide explainability for LightGBM because the explainability output is
easy to interpret and has been successfully applied in treeôÙbased gradient boosting models as
mentioned in Section 1.3.5.
The TFT is chosen as the final model to evaluate. Considering forecasting performance, the TFT
is designed explicitly for multiôÙhorizon probabilistic forecasts and outperforms all other considered
models on the electric and traffic datasets. The model also uses the direct forecasting method,
which is argued to be more suitable for multiôÙhorizon forecasting as mentioned in Section 1.3.2.
The TFT has builtôÙin explainability, which arguably is preferable as no extra computation is needed.
The variableôÙwise and temporal importance can, in combination, inform the customer of WoodôÙ
sense which features are important at what times. The TFT can give local and global explanaôÙ
tions. However, it is the modelãs local explainability that makes it interesting. This is because it
can provide the customers of Woodsense explanations for each sensor. The IMVôÙLSTM can also
provide local explainability. Therefore, it was also considered due to its good performance in the
benchmarking dataset for explainable forecasting [65]. However, the IMVôÙLSTM is not tested in
providing multiôÙhorizon probabilistic forecasts. Therefore, it is not chosen.
An additional argument for TFT is that it is designed to use covariate static data to condition the
networkãs temporal dynamics and variable importance. This is believed to make the model learn
general behavior globally while also adapting forecasts to a specific sensor.
To summarize, TFT seems to be the best model because of its good forecasting performances,
builtôÙin variableôÙwise and temporal explainability, and handling of covariate data, which is believed
to be particularly useful on the Woodsense data.
Stochastic differential equations and modelôÙbased deep learning were briefly considered. It was
evaluated that stochastic differential equations would be too challenging to adapt to each sensor,
and there is insufficient time and domain knowledge to develop a modelôÙbased deep learning
model. In Appendix A.6 the methods and reasons for not choosing them are elaborated.
1.5
Project Aim
This thesis is a feasibility study that explores to what extent useful probabilistic explainable moisôÙ
ture content forecasts can be created with a dataôÙdriven approach.
8
Exploring Machine Learning Methods for Forecasting IoT Measurements in Wooden Structures
==PAGE:18==
Recent works in the machine learning research community have been focused on explainability
and multiôÙhorizon probabilistic forecasts, which results in new models that are stateôÙofôÙtheôÙart.
Additionally, machine learning methods are wellôÙsuited to multivariate highôÙvolume data [9, 15, 33]
as the data of Woodsense is. Therefore, this thesis explores how the LightGBM model and TFT
perform with respect to forecasting performance and explainability correctness. Their performance
is compared to the VAR and SARIMAX model.
The best model concerning forecasting and explainability will be trained and tested on sensor data
and historic weather data. It will be explored if the model obtains better forecasts with respect to
a naive baseline model that assumes the moisture content is constant for the next 14 days. The
model will be tested with both historic and forecasted weather data.
The usefulness of the forecasts will be evaluated concerning how they perform compared to a
naive baseline. The applicability of the explainable forecasts is assessed with chosen forecasting
examples to evaluate how they can be utilized. Finally, the feasibility of using the model will be
discussed with respect to the usefulness of the explainable forecasts and implementation costs
(training and inference costs of the model).
To evaluate explainability correctness, the models are evaluated on synthetic datasets from the
forecasting explainability benchmarking paper mentioned in Section 1.3.6. The models are evalôÙ
uated on correctly determining variable dependence and lags. VAR is used as a baseline model
because it is fitting for the synthetic multivariate autoregressive datasets.
The publicly available PM2.5 dataset3 is used to evaluate the forecasting performance.
The
dataset is similar to the Woodsense data concerning the target values being dependent on huôÙ
midity, temperature, and rain. A public dataset is chosen because the forecasting results can be
benchmarked with other models (mentioned in Section 1.3.4) to verify that the models perform
well. SARIMAX with AutoôÙARIMA is used as a baseline model.
To summarize, this thesis is a feasibility study of applying stateôÙofôÙtheôÙart machine learning methôÙ
ods to achieve useful probabilistic explainable moisture content forecasts. The main aim of the
thesis is to develop a model with explainable forecasts that are useable for Woodsense and its cusôÙ
tomers. For that reason, reducing implementation costs is not a factor considered in the choice
of models. Also, the aim of obtaining useful and correct explanations is secondary to accurate
forecasts since good explanations do not give any value if the forecasts are wrong.
1.6
Research Questions
Based on the project aim, the following research questions are defined.
1. Can LightGBM with SHAP, and the TFT provide explainability with comparable quality to
the VAR model concerning correctly identifying variable dependence and lag on a synthetic
multivariate dataset?
2. To what extent can LightGBM and TFT achieve better multiôÙhorizon probabilistic forecasts
than SARIMAX on the PM2.5 dataset concerning root mean square error, mean absolute
error, and 0.1 and 0.9 quantile loss?
3. Based on the evaluated explainability and multiôÙhorizon forecasting capabilities, is LightGBM
with SHAP or the TFT most likely to be suitable for providing useful explainable moisture
content forecasts?
4. Is it feasible to achieve 14ôÙday forecasts of moisture content using weather forecasts that
outperform a naive baseline model concerning root mean square error and mean absolute
error?
Furthermore, it will be discussed if it is feasible for Woodsense to use the forecasts with respect
to how informative the explanations are and if the model can be implemented concerning training
and inference time.

1.7
Overview
This thesis is arranged as follows. Chapter 2 describes the theory related to the models and
methods applied in this thesis. Chapter 3 explains the general methods that are applied in the
experiments. Chapter 4 describes the data, methods, and results specific to the explainability
evaluation experiment. Similarly, Chapter 5 describes the data, methods, and results specific to
the forecasting performance experiments. The chapter also includes a discussion of the choice of
model to develop to forecast moisture content. Chapter 6 explains the data, methods, and results
specific to the development of a model to forecast moisture content. The chapter also includes a
discussion of the limitations of methods, propositions for further work, and the feasibility of applying
the model in production. Finally, Chapter 7 presents the conclusions for this thesis and a summary
of propositions for further work.

2
Theory
The following chapter will describe the theory of the models mentioned in the previous chapter
applied in this thesis. Section 2.1 explains quantile loss and evaluation functions. Section 2.2 and
Section 2.3 explain the VAR model and SARIMAX model. Theory associated with LightGBM is exôÙ
plained in Section 2.4. Section 2.5 explains the postôÙhoc explainability method SHAP. Section 2.6
explains theory associated to deep learning and will provide background knowledge for the TFT.
Section 2.7 explains the TFT architecture. Finally, in Section 2.8 theory regarding hyperparameter
optimization and bayesian optimization is explained.
The VAR and SARIMAX models are described superficially since they are baseline models. It is
assumed the reader has basic knowledge of machine learning and time series analysis, such as
stochastic processes, stationarity, and backshift operators.
2.1
Training and Evaluation Functions
The following section describes loss functions used for training and evaluation. The quantile loss
function is used as a cost function for the LightGBM and TFT. Root mean square error (RMSE) is
used as a cost function for the VAR and SARIMAX model. Mean absolute error (MAE) is solely
used as an evaluation function.
Quantile loss or pinball loss is a loss function first introduced in 1979 to perform quantile regression
by Koenker et al. [45]. Koenker et al. show that optimizing the quantile loss results in correct
quantiles. Quantile loss can be expressed in numerous ways. The definition in Equation 2.1 is
based on the definition used by the TFT paper [42].
QL(y, ùy, q) = q(y ãùy)+ + (1 ãq)(ùy ãy)+

In Figure 2.1, the 0.1, 0.5 and 0.9 quantile loss for residuals in the interval [ã6, 6] are shown.
In a 0.1 quantile forecast, it is expected that 90% of the forecasts are below the true values.
In Figure 2.1, QL0.1 loss penalizes positive residuals (i.e., predictions above the true value) more
than negative residuals. Intuitively, a model trained on QL0.1 will try to minimize positive residuals.

2.3
SARIMAX
The following section will shortly describe SARIMAX [68] and AutoôÙARIMA [14]. The SARIMAX
model is an extension of the ARIMA model for describing a time series with multiplicative seasonal
behavior and exogenous covariates. Exogenous covariates are not affected by other variables in
the system. Simply put, the covariate x does effect y but y does not affect x.
The ARIMA model will shortly be explained, and afterward, it will be shown how the model is
extended to SARIMAX. ARIMA models univariate time series where the underlying realization is
assumed to be a stochastic process [44]. Specifically, the differentiated time series is assumed to
be an ARMA process. An ARIMA(p, d, q) model can be expressed with backshift operators as
üp(L)ãdyt = ö¡q(L)üçt
(2.6)
where p denotes the order (number of time lags) of the AR process, q denotes the order of a MA
process, and d is the degree of differencing (i.e., how many times the time series is differenced).
The SARIMAX(p, d, q) û (P, D, Q)s model can be expressed as regression with SARIMA errors
[68], as can be seen in Equation 2.7.
yt = xtöýt + ut
üp(L)ùüP (Ls)ãdãDut = ö¡q(L)ùö¡Q(Ls)üçt
(2.7)
xt is a 1 û K vector of covariates and öý is its coefficient vector and ut denotes the error which
is assumed to follow a SARIMA process. The P, D, and Q respectively denote the order for the
12

==PAGE:22==
seasonal AR, differencing, and MA orders. The periodicity of the data is denoted with s, often
equivalent to days, weeks, months, or years. If the SARIMAX model explains the underlying
process well, then the residuals (üçt) should be white noise.
OLS can estimate the exogenous variables. The SARIMAX model is in this thesis implemented
with the statsmodels python package1. The implementation writes the SARIMA model in stateôÙ
space form. It estimates the coefficients by maximum likelihood via Kalman filter2. The weights
are initialised using ConditionalôÙsumôÙofôÙsquares [69]. More details can be read in Chapter 3.6.2
in ãTime Series Analysis by State Space Methodsã by Durbin and Koopman [68].
OneôÙstep forecast is the conditional mean ùyt+h|t = E(yt+h|xt, yt, ytã1, ..., y0) [44]. The prediction
intervals are calculated with respect to the standard deviation of the prediction error (üçt) such
that ùyt+h|t ôÝ zöÝ/2üüç where zöÝ/2 is the öÝ/2 quantile in the standard normal distribution. MultiôÙstep
forecasts are done with the iterative method as described in Section 1.3.2.
2.3.1
AutoôÙARIMA
AutoôÙARIMA is used to determine the optimal order, seasonal order, and degree of differencing
[14]. Shortly put, the AutoôÙARIMA algorithm iteratively tests different model orders and chooses
the best model based on Akaike Information Criterion (AIC) [70].
The AutoôÙARIMA algorithm first determines the degree of differencing needed with KPSS testing
for nonôÙseasonal differencing (d) [71] and CanovaôÙHansen test for seasonal differencing (D) [72].
Afterward, the algorithm first tries four model order combinations where the best model with the
lowest AIC is chosen as the ãcurrent modelã. Then the algorithm makes stepôÙwise changes to
the ãcurrent modelã. If a new model obtains lower AIC, it becomes the ãcurrent modelã, and the
procedure is repeated. The procedure stops when up to 13 variations do not lead to lower AIC, and
the ãcurrent modelã is chosen as the best model. In Appendix B.1, the full AutoôÙARIMA algorithm
is provided.
2.4
LightGBM
The following section will describe the theory relevant to understanding the LightGBM model [36].
The LightGBM model is a decision treeôÙbased gradient boosting model. Therefore, decision trees
and gradient boosting are firstly described. Afterward, the XGBoost model [38] is explained beôÙ
cause it is a precursor for the LightGBM model. Then the LightGBM model is presented with
a focus on how it differs from XGBoost. XGBoost and LightGBM have many details regarding
optimization of training speed, but this section will focus on the aspects of the model which are
relevant to model performance. All theory in this section is limited to regression tasks. In Chapter
3, it will be explained how LightGBM is applied for time series forecasting.
2.4.1
Decision Trees
It is assumed that the reader has basic knowledge of decision trees. Therefore, decision trees for
regression will be explained superficially. Decision trees for regression tasks are called regression
trees. Theory and notation are based on Chapter 9 in ãThe elements of statistical learning: data
mining, inference, and predictionã by Hastie et al. [73].
Decision trees partition observations to a set of leaf nodes in a binary tree by recursively doing
binary splits based on the features of an observation. By partitioning the observations into M
leaf nodes R1, R2, ..., RM and providing each leaf node with a constant response cm, then the
prediction of the decision tree is defined as
f(x) =

partition where the optimal cm is the average y values in Rm. The best binary partition is deterôÙ

Growing a complete decision tree can easily lead to overfitting because predictions can be based
on a single observation. To minimize the risk of overfitting, hyperparameters are used to control
the size of the tree. The hyperparameters are ãmaximum number of leaf nodesã and a ãminimum
number of observations in a leaf nodeã. Lower values of ãmaximum number of leaf nodesã and
higher values of ãminimum number of observations in a lead nodeã lead to smaller trees and less
overfitting.
2.4.2

Gradient boosting combines an ensemble of weak base learners that usually are decision trees
[73]. The training algorithm is introduced by Jerome H. Friedman [74]. A prediction output ùyi given

The gradient boosting model introduces the ãnumber of estimatorsã as an extra hyperparameter.
It follows that increasing the number of estimators can lead to overfitting.
Gradient boosting models has the limitation that they only can produce oneôÙdimensional outputs.
Thus, if M outputs are needed, it is necessary to train M models.
2.4.3

2.4.4
LightGBM
The LightGBM model is also developed by Microsoft and mainly focuses on optimizing the effiôÙ
ciency and scalability such that gradient boosting can be applied to highôÙvolume data with many
features [36]. This is accomplished by two techniques which are named ãGradientôÙbased OneôÙside
Samplingã (GOSS) and ãExclusive Feature Bundlingã (EFB). As the focus primarily is on aspects
concerning prediction performance, the optimization techniques are shortly described, and the
reader is referred to the paper for more details.
GOSS excludes a significant proportion of data instances with small gradients and uses the rest to
estimate information gain. GOSS arguably affects forecasting performance because the model is
trained on a subset of samples. However, GOSS is not applied in this thesis. EFB bundles mutually
exclusive features (i.e., they rarely take nonzero values simultaneously) to reduce the number of
features, speeding up the consideration of each possible split. The LightGBM also further speeds
up training using gradient binning. Shortly put, this is done by binning similar features into the
16
Exploring Machine Learning Methods for Forecasting IoT Measurements in Wooden Structures
==PAGE:26==
same bins and summing each binãs gradients and hessians. Then the splits can be considered on
each bin instead of each observation.
...
...
Level-wise tree growth
Leaf-wise tree growth
Figure 2.4: Illustration of level and leafôÙwise tree growth. The levelôÙwise tree growth creates splits
of leaf nodes by traversing each level of a tree from left to right. The leafôÙwise tree growth splits
the leave with maximum loss reduction among the considered leaves. Inspiration for the figure is
from ?iiTb,ffHB;?i;#KX`2/i?2/Q+bXBQf2MfpjXjXkf62im`2bX?iKH.
The main difference of LightGBM is it uses leafôÙwise treeôÙgrowth [75] contrary to XGBoost that
uses levelôÙwise tree growth [36] as illustrated in Figure 2.4. LevelôÙwise tree growth creates splits
of leaf nodes by traversing each level of a tree from left to right. LeafôÙwise splits the leaf with
maximum loss reduction among the considered leaves. The leafôÙwise approach can easier result
in overfitting, so a hyperôÙparameter controlling the maximum depth is included.
LightGBM also introduces an öÝ regularization parameter in the loss function that regularizes the
weights by öÝ||w||.
Hyperparameters of LightGBM
The hyperparameters of LightGBM introduced throughout this section are summarized below.
Many of the hyperparameters mutually affect each other. For example, ãmaximum depthã, ãminiôÙ
mum number of child samplesã, ãnumber of leavesã and ãö°ã all regularizes the size of the tree. HowôÙ
ever, they regularize in different ways. Therefore, they are all used in hyperparameter optimization
as it is hypothesized some forecasting tasks benefits from different forms of regularization.
ãÂ Learning rate: Learning rate is also referred to as shrinkage or öñ. Controls the ratio of which
the next decision tree contributes to the forecast. Higher learning rate is more likely to lead
to overfitting.
ãÂ Number of estimators: Number of estimators/base learners to use in gradient boosting.
More estimators is more likely to lead to overfitting
ãÂ Maximum depth: Maximum depth of the decision trees. Deeper trees are more likely to
overfit.
ãÂ Minimum number of child samples: Minimum number of observations in a leaf node. The
model is more likely to overfit with low number of allowed child samples.
ãÂ Maximum number of leaves: Number of leaf nodes. Higher number of leaves makes
overfitting more likely.
ãÂ Subsample ratio: Sampling ratio of observations. Low ratios will lead to less overfitting.
ãÂ Column sample ratio range: Sampling ratio of features. Less ratios will lead to less overôÙ
fitting.
ãÂ öÝ regularization: L1 regularization parameter for amplitude of output (w)
ãÂ ö£ regularization: L2 regularization parameter for amplitude of leaf node output (w).
ãÂ ö° regularization: Regularization parameter for number of leaf nodes. Practically controls
the minimum loss reduction to create a split.
Exploring Machine Learning Methods for Forecasting IoT Measurements in Wooden Structures
17
==PAGE:27==
2.5
SHAP
Shapley Additive Explanations (SHAP) is an explainability method to provide local explainability
of model predictions [62]. The SHAP values are based on Shapley values [76]. Shapley values
originate from game theory and estimate the contribution of a player (feature) to the output (preôÙ
diction) of the game (model). A playerãs contribution is the weighted average of the change in
outcome when introducing the player in all possible permutations with other players.
SHAP values explain how a feature attribute to a change of a prediction by an explanation model
defined as
g(zôÇ) = ü0 +
M
%
i=1
üiziôÇ
(2.19)
where ziôÇ ã{0, 1}M, M is number of features and üi ãR. ziôÇ represents a feature xi being present
(ziôÇ = 1) or not present (ziôÇ = 0), and üi is the feature attribution of feature xi. ü0 is the expected
value of the target value with no features present. Thus, SHAP values explain how to get from
the base value E[f(z)] that is the expected value with no features to the current output f(x) as
illustrated in Figure 2.5. The order of adding the features matters if the model is nonôÙlinear or if
the features are not independent. Therefore, the SHAP values are calculated by averaging the üi
values across all possible permutations.
Figure 2.5: Illustration shows how observations x1, x2 and x3 contribute to the final prediction of
f(x) with respect to the expected value with no features. üi denotes the SHAP value for xi. üi can
depend on the order the features are added. Therefore, üi is averaged across all possible perôÙ
mutations. The figure is from ãA Unified Approach to Interpreting Model Predictionsã by Lundberg
and Lee [62].
It would be necessary to train 2M models to calculate the exact Shapley values where M is the
number of features that quickly becomes infeasible. The SHAP paper introduces approximation
methods that applies perturbations to the features which mimic the feature that is missing [62].
However, in treeôÙbased models, it is not needed to make perturbations to the input. Instead, the
tree can be traversed recursively to estimate the contribution of a set of features to a prediction
[77]. Given an observation with a subset of feature values, the contribution of these features is
the proportion of training examples in the subôÙtrees traversed due to the features, times the accuôÙ
mulated output of all leaf nodes of the subôÙtrees. The exact algorithm can be found in Appendix
B.2. It should be noted SHAP uses an optimized algorithm that runs in O(TLD2) time instead of
the algorithm given in the appendix that runs in O(TL2M). T is the number of trees, L is the maxôÙ
imum number of leaves in a tree and D is for balanced trees log L. The algorithm is not described
because it only improves speed of the evaluation.
2.6
Deep Learning Architectures and Training
This section will describe the basic aspects of Deep Learning as well as architectures used in the
TFT. Thus, the section will provide background knowledge of the TFT.
2.6.1
Multilayer Perceptron
Multilayer Perceptron (MLP) is the simplest form of neural network. It is modeled after the human
brain where neurons are activated by connected nodes if a threshold value is reached [78]. The
thresholds are governed by activation functions which are explained shortly.
18
Exploring Machine Learning Methods for Forecasting IoT Measurements in Wooden Structures
==PAGE:28==
MLP consists of stacked layers where the first and last layers are called the input and output layers.
The layers between the input and output layers are called hidden layers. Each layer (except for
the input layer) consists of multiple neurons that take input from the former layer and transforms it
as input to the next layer. When all outputs from one layer are transferred to all the neurons in the
next layer it is called fullyôÙconnected. In Figure 2.6, an MLP with two hidden layers is illustrated.

2.6.2
Backpropagation
Neural networks are trained by backpropagation which is done by stochastic gradient descent.
The weights and biases (ö¡) are initialized randomly and the loss is calculated as a differentiable
function of the parameters L(ö¡). The weights are updated with respect to the gradient of the loss
function as shown in Equation 2.21.
ö¡t = ö¡tã1 ãöñãö¡L(ö¡tã1)
(2.21)
The gradients for each weight with respect to the loss are calculated using the chainôÙrule and are
called a backward pass. öñ is called the learning rate and is a hyperparameter that determines the
magnitude of the change of weights. Calculating the loss for a single sequence results in high
variance and calculating the loss for the whole training set will make training very slow. Therefore,
the gradient is calculated from randomly selected miniôÙbatches that are subsets of the training data.
The size of the miniôÙbatches is another hyperparameter that needs to be tuned. The procedure of
updating the weights is called a backward pass.
2.6.3
Activation Functions
An activation function is a function that takes a number as input and transforms it into another
number. An activation function should have a wellôÙdefined derivative and be nonôÙlinear. If it is
linear, two chained linear functions can be expressed with a single linear function. There exists
multiple activation functions [78] but this section will only explain the sigmoid, softmax, the Rectified
Exploring Machine Learning Methods for Forecasting IoT Measurements in Wooden Structures
19
==PAGE:29==

the TFT. The sigmoid function often denoted with ü is the first activation function applied in neural
networks and transforms the input to values in the interval [0, 1].
ü(x) =

2.6.4
Long ShortôÙTerm Memory
The Long ShortôÙTerm Memory (LSTM) cell is a Recurrent Neural Network (RNN) [27]. An RNN
processes sequential information by keeping an internal hidden state (h) of earlier information that
is recursively passed and updated between input sequences as shown in Figure 2.7. A vanilla
Elman RNN [80] would take the form
ht = hh(Whxt + Uhhtã1 + bh)
yt = hy(Wyht + by)
(2.26)
where Wh processes the new input, Uh processes the former hidden state and hh and hy are
activation functions. At each time step, the hidden state is transformed by Wy and hy to create
a prediction yt. The RNN can have many inputôÙoutput variants which are manyôÙtoôÙone which is
used as encoders or oneôÙtoôÙmany and manyôÙtoôÙmany which are used as decoders. ManyôÙtoôÙone
has multiple inputs but only makes one output which is the last hidden state ht. OneôÙtoôÙmany has
a single input but creates outputs for each step. ManyôÙtoôÙmany has an input and output for each
computation state.
Vanilla RNNs can struggle to maintain longôÙterm memory of past information [16]. The LSTM is
introduced to better maintain longôÙterm memory by introducing a cell state ct which stores longôÙ
term information which is modelled through a input gate (it), last gate (gt), forget gate (ft) and
output gate (ot) [27, 78]. The forget gate controls what information is removed from the previous
state and the input gate along with the last gate controls information to be added. The output gate
controls what information from the cell state should be outputted [78]. The structure of the network
20

vector to the next time step. Inspiration for the figure is from ãTime Series Forecasting with Deep
Learning: A Surveyã [16].
is shown in Equations 2.27ôÙ2.32. The LSTM has many consecutive steps of multiplication. This
can lead to exploding gradients that can move the parameters extremely far from a minimum [78].
This is usually mitigated by using gradient clipping where the gradient is ãclippedã to a threshold
value if it exceeds that value [78].

2.6.5
MultiôÙHead Attention
The development of attention has led to further improvements in learning longôÙterm dependencies
[16] where the transformer architecture using multiôÙhead attention by Vaswani et al. achieves
stateôÙofôÙtheôÙart performance in NLP tasks [81].
Attention layers allow the network to focus on significant time steps in the past directly (even far
back in time) by using dynamically generated weights and has been shown to work well in learning
longôÙterm dependencies [16]. This is shown in Figure 2.8 where different weights on each time
step are generated for a time step. These attention weights can also be used for interpretability as
they provide information on what time steps are important for a prediction. This section focuses
on describing multiôÙhead attention as introduced by Vaswani et al. [81].

as input where Q and K are N ûdk and V is N ûdv. N is the number of sequential steps. ãScaled

Attention without prior sequential processing cannot infer the sequential order of the observations.
Positional encoding is applied to the inputs to enable the attention to infer the sequential order.
The positional encoding for time step t is a dmodelôÙdimensional vector that contains a unique set
of encodings. In Appendix B.3, positional encoding is visualized.
2.6.6
Entity embeddings
Entity embeddings transform categorical variables to embedding vectors which makes it easier
for neural networks to interpret [82]. Simply put, entity embeddings can be viewed as a dictionary
that maps categorical variables to a vector space. The vectors are randomly initialized and can
be interpreted as conditioned bias vectors. The embeddings are trained using stochastic gradient
descent the same way as bias vectors.
2.6.7
Training and Regularization Methods
The following section describes theory associated with training the TFT.
Adam Optimizer
The Adam optimizer proposed by Kingma et al. [83] is used to tune the weights of the TFT. The
Adam optimizer is argued to be wellôÙsuited for highôÙvolume data in highôÙdimensional parameter
space. The Adam optimizer introduces an individual learning rate for each parameter and a moôÙ
mentum update. The Adam algorithm is at update step t defined as
ö¡t = ö¡tã1 ãöñ
ùmt

(2.36)

where üç = 10ã8 is for numerical stability. The algorithm updates the gradient with exponential
moving averages and squared exponential decay. Kingma et al. proposes öý1 = 0.9 and öý2 = 0.999
as good default parameters [83] which are used in this thesis.
Learning Rate Decay
During training can the weight updates be around a steep valley or oscillate between walls of a
local minimum [84]. In these cases, it can be beneficial to reduce the learning rate. This is called
learning rate decay and can be implemented in different ways. One way is to schedule the learning
rate with a decay function. This method requires the parameters of the decay function to be tuned.
Another method is to reduce the learning rate with a factor when learning plateaus. The second
method is utilized in this thesis where the learning rate is halved when validation loss does not
improve after a specified number of epochs.
Early Stopping
Early stopping can be applied to avoid overfitting [78]. The idea of early stopping is to stop training
when the model starts to overfit on the validation set. During training is a copy of each model
saved and the best model on the validation set is then returned. Early stopping is applied using
ãpatienceã and ãminimum improvementã. For example, if ãpatienceã is set to 10 and ãminimum
improvementã to 0.001, then the model stops training if the validation loss does not improve by more
than 0.001 after 10 validations. Afterward, the model with the lowest validation loss is returned.
The early stopping parameters can be challenging to determine since the validation loss potentially
can improve after the model is stopped. This is especially the case if the training loss has not
converged.
Exploring Machine Learning Methods for Forecasting IoT Measurements in Wooden Structures
23
==PAGE:33==
Dropout
Dropout is a regularization technique that randomly sets elements of the input vector of a hidden
layer to zero during training. Each element in the input vector has a probability p of being set to
zero. p is a hyperparameter that needs to be tuned where higher levels of p are more effective
against overfitting.

Basically, dropout results in multiple different models that are trained and combined during inferôÙ

Layer Normalization
Layer normalization [85] is a simple technique that normalizes the output of the neurons in each
layer to a mean of zero and a variance of one. It has been found to improve the training time and
generalization performance in RNN models by stabilizing the hidden state dynamics [85]. The
layer normalization statistics for layer l are calculated as follows

Layer normalization was developed after batch normalization [86] to enable normalization on
RNNs. Batch normalization improved training time and generalization of neural networks by reôÙ
ducing internal covariate shift. However, the normalization was performed per batch that neglects
the recurrent part of the network which is why layer normalization was proposed.
2.7
Temporal Fusion Transformer
The following section will describe the Temporal Fusion Transformer (TFT) developed by Lim et al.
[42]. The TFT is developed to provide explainable probabilistic multiôÙhorizon forecasts. Forecasts
are provided with the direct sequence2sequence method where a manyôÙtoôÙone encoder takes
all values known until inference time as input. The output of the encoder is passed to a manyôÙ
toôÙmany decoder that takes known future values as input and creates a forecast for each time
step. Additionally, the TFT can be conditioned by taking static covariates as input. In the case
of Woodsense, static covariates can be static information about a sensor such as the ID of the
sensor. In Figure 2.11, it is illustrated how the TFT creates forecasts with static covariates and
observed and known inputs.
The TFT uses a Variable Selection Network to select relevant inputs at each timestep. FurtherôÙ
more, the TFT uses multiôÙhead attention to attend to important time steps. The multiôÙhead attention
is modified such that it is more explainable which shortly will be described in more detail.
24
Exploring Machine Learning Methods for Forecasting IoT Measurements in Wooden Structures
==PAGE:34==
Figure 2.11: Illustration of how TFT creates forecasts by using observed and known future inputs
and static covariates. The figure is from ãTemporal Fusion Transformers for Interpretable MultiôÙ
horizon Time Series Forecastingã [42].
2.7.1
Architecture
The architecture of TFT is shown in Figure 2.12. As can be seen, the TFT consists of many
subôÙcomponents. The components of the TFT architecture is Variable Selection Network, LSTM
encoder/decoder, Gated Residual Network (GRN), Masked Interpretable MultiôÙhead Attention and
Static Covariate Encoders. Each of these will be components will be described shortly.
The TFT provides forecasts in the following way. The TFT first applies variable selection on each
of the variables which then is passed to an LSTM encoder where the hidden and cell state are
conditioned on the static covariates. The cell and hidden state are passed to the LSTM decoder
that only takes future values as input. The outputs of the LSTM cells at each step is passed to a
GRN and afterward passed to a Masked Interpretable MultiôÙhead Attention layer. The parts of the
attention output that are for future time steps are passed to another GRN which then is fed into a
fullyôÙconnected/dense layer for quantile forecasts. The TFT also uses multiple skip connections
that are normalized with layer normalization to enable the model to skip over unused components
of the network.
Lim et al. argue that the combination of LSTM cells and attention allows the model to learn both
longôÙ and shortôÙterm temporal relationships [42]. The LSTM learns shortôÙterm relationships by
ãlocalã processing and the attention captures longôÙterm dependencies.
As shown in Figure 2.12, the TFT is configured with a static lookôÙback window k and forecasting
horizon ümax. The value of ümax depends on how far it is of interest to forecast. k, on the other
hand, is a hyperparameter as too short k does not have enough prior information and a too long
k results in unnecessary inference time and less training examples.
Gated Residual Network
According to Lim et al., the Gated Residual Network (GRN) is developed with the motivation of
giving the model the flexibility to apply nonôÙlinear processing when needed. Thus, it is providing
adaptive depth and network complexity. The GRN is defined in Equations 2.42ôÙ2.45 and visualized
in Figure 2.13.

Variable Selection Network
The Variable Selection Network has all variables transformed to dmodelôÙdimensional vectors. CatôÙ
egorical variables are transformed via entity embeddings and continuous variables are linearly
transformed. According to the authors, the transformation of the continuous variables is done
because the dmodelôÙdimensional vectors then will match subsequent layers for skip connections.
As shown in Figure 2.12, the static, observed and known inputs have separate variable selection
performed. However, the same architecture applies to all three inputs.
In Figure 2.14, the architecture of the Variable Selection Network is shown. The network performs
variable selection at time step t by sending dmodelôÙdimensional vectors (öƒ1

The vXt vectors can be extracted from each time step to learn how the model attends to the
features at each time step. It should be noted that the Variable Selection Network does not take any
temporal dependencies into consideration which can lead to the variables are chosen concerning
their global importance.
Static Covariate Encoders
The Static Covariate Encoders are applied after static variable selection. Defining öÑ to be the
output from static variable selection, the Static Variate Encoder provides static encoding by c =
GRN(öÑ). There is in total four Static Variate Encoder networks that creates the context vectors cs,
ce, cc and ch. The vectors respectively provide context for variable selection, LSTM encoder (cell
and hidden state), and the output of the LSTM cells.

2.7.3
Hyperparameters of Temporal Fusion Transformer
The hyperparameter search range used for hyperparameter optimization for each model are listed
below:
ãÂ Learning rate: Controls the rate of updating the weights. Too high learning rate results in
unstable training and too low results in model struggles to converge.
ãÂ Gradient clip value: Regularization against exploding gradients. Clips the gradients to the
specified value.
ãÂ Hidden size: Size of the vectors passed through the network. Also called dmodel.
ãÂ Number of attention heads: Number of attention heads applied in attention.
ãÂ Number of LSTM layers: Number of LSTM layers.
ãÂ Dropout: Probability of setting inputs of hidden layers to zero. Higher probabilities result in
higher regularization.
2.8
Hyperparameter Optimization
Hyperparameter optimization is the process of finding optimal hyperparameters of a model conôÙ
cerning minimizing the validation loss. There are many algorithms for hyperparameter optimizaôÙ
tion. The most common are ãManualã, ãGrid Searchã, ãRandom Searchã, and ãBayesian OptimizaôÙ
tionã.
With a large complex model and many hyperparameters, it is computationally expensive to do hyôÙ
perparameter optimization as a model needs to be trained with each set of hyperparameters and
28
Exploring Machine Learning Methods for Forecasting IoT Measurements in Wooden Structures
==PAGE:38==

evaluated on a validation set. Therefore, the manual method is often not possible to do in practice
[87]. Grid or Random Search are more useful as the train and evaluation cycle can be automatiôÙ
cally looped [88]. However, these methods are very inefficient as the choice of hyperparameters
are not based on prior trials [87]. Thus, the algorithms unnecessarily try bad hyperparameter
combinations.
Bayesian Optimization on the other hand bases the choice of hyperparameters on prior knowledge
of hyperparameter trials. Simply put, Bayesian Optimization uses a probabilistic model to estimate
the probability of a score given a set of hyperparameters [88]. By maximizing the expected imôÙ
provement with respect to the hyperparameters (i.e. how much will the score improve given these
hyperparameters), informed hyperparameter trials can be conducted.
Due to time constraints, bayesian optimization is used for hyperparameter optimization using the
python package Optuna [89]. As a default, Optuna uses the TreeôÙStructured Parzen Estimator
(TPE) sampler to create hyperparameter trials which also is applied in this thesis. Defining x to
denote the hyperparameter set and y to denote the loss, TPE models p(x|y) by the likelihood
functions l(x) and g(x) in the following way
p(x|y) =

of hyperparameters that gives the lowest ratio in g(x)

Therefore, the set of hyperparameters that have high probability in l(x) and low probability in
g(x) is wanted. Intuitively, this makes sense as we would prefer to sample from l(x) where the
hyperparameters are below yã. How the algorithm moves towards a local minimum is illustrated
in Figure 2.15.

3
Methods
This chapter describes the general methods applied in the experiments in the following chapters.
Details specific to each experiment are explained in their respective chapters.
Section 3.1 describes the complete experiment setup to provide an overview of how the experiôÙ
ments are connected. Details concerning how the models are evaluated and compared are exôÙ
plained in each experiment. Section 3.2 describes how the training, validation, and test sets are
formed. The details of how each dataset is split will be explained in the following chapters. Section
3.3 explains how machine learning models are trained in forecasting tasks. Section 3.4 specifiôÙ
cally describes how LightGBM is implemented for forecasting tasks. Finally, Section 3.5 explains
implementation of the models.
A link to the code used in this thesis is provided in Appendix C.1.
3.1
Experiment Setup
The complete experiment setup can be seen in Figure 3.1. In total, there are three experiments
ãExplainability evaluation on a synthetic datasetã, ãForecasting evaluation on PM2.5 datasetã, and
ãMoisture content forecasting on Woodsense dataã. All models are trained to provide probabilistic
forecasts in the interval [0.1, 0.9]. This span was chosen since it is used in several literature [42,
29, 90]. Also, evaluation on only two quantiles makes the evaluation more succinct.
The general setup for the explainability and forecasting experiments are very similar. LightGBM
and Temporal Fusion Transformer are tested in both experiments. As mentioned in Section 1.5, the
VAR is used as a baseline model in the explainability experiment because the modelãs assumptions
are fitting to the synthetic datasets. SARIMAX is applied to the forecasting experiments because it
is well understood and explainable. SARIMAX can also take exogenous covariates as input which
is useful for the PM2.5 data.
Hyperparameter optimization is required to compare the models fairly. In both experiments, hyperôÙ
parameter optimization was performed by evaluating models on a validation dataset. The VAR has
no hyperparameters as p was predetermined, so hyperparameter optimization is not necessary.
The SARIMAX has applied AutoôÙARIMA to decide the order of the model. Each model with the
best hyperparameters is retrained on the combined training and validation set and then evaluated
on a holdôÙout test set to estimate the generalization error.
Early stopping and learning rate decay cannot be utilized while training on the combined training
and validation set, as this would create a biased estimate on the test set. Instead, the Temporal
Fusion Transformer is trained with the same number of epochs as the model with the best hyôÙ
perparameters in hyperparameter optimization. That means if the model is stopped due to early
stopping, then the epochs are equal to when early stopping is activated. Naturally, this does not
guarantee training stops at the minimum loss. However, it makes the test set error less biased.
Learning rate decay is manually set with a halving of the learning rate at the same epochs as
during training on the training set.
The models are compared on the test set to estimate how they generalize concerning explainability
and forecasting performance. Thus, the two experiments aim to jointly determine what model
will most likely perform well in providing accurate explainable forecasts on the Woodsense data.
Forecasting performance is weighted higher than explainability since it is assessed that correct
forecasts provide more value to the customer of Woodsense than correct explainability. It was
chosen to make the comparisons on public datasets instead of the Woodsense data because it
is possible to compare the models with other literature. In this way, it is possible to ensure the
developed models are wellôÙtrained and sound choices concerning multiôÙhorizon forecasting and
explainability are made. It was chosen to only develop one model for moisture content due to time
constraints in this project.
Exploring Machine Learning Methods for Forecasting IoT Measurements in Wooden Structures

==PAGE:41==
Normally, model selection is solely performed on the validation set and the test set is used to get
an unbiased estimate of the generalization error. However, it was decided to base the choice of
the final model for moisture content forecasting on the test sets. This is because it is assessed to
mitigate the risk of making a biased choice. Also, the Woodsense dataset can be viewed as the
final test set for the model.

The chosen model has feature selection and hyperparameter optimization performed on the valôÙ

weather data (i.e., true weather data) and forecasted weather data to evaluate if any improvements
are necessary prior to the model being tested on the test set.

respect to forecasting performance with both historic and forecasted weather data on holdôÙout test
