Details of the explainability experiments are provided in Chapter 4 and details of the forecasting
experiment are explained in Chapter 5. In Chapter 6, the development of the final model for
moisture content forecasting is explained.
3.2
Training, Validation and Test Splits
The datasets are split into training, validation, and test sets. It is of interest to explore how the
models perform on future unseen data, so the validation and test sets are the last segments of the
time series. Figure 3.2 illustrates how the splits are made.
KôÙfold crossôÙvalidation (CV) is arguably better during model selection, and estimation of generôÙ
alization error as the risk of randomly performing well on a dataset is mitigated. CV is often not
used in time series settings because it does not account for serial correlation and nonôÙstationary
data, which is why leaveôÙoneôÙout is usually applied to evaluate models in time series settings.
However, in ãA note on the validity of crossôÙvalidation for evaluating autoregressive time series
predictionã by Bergmeir, Hyndman, and Koo [91], the authors propose a variant of CV named
nonôÙdependent crossôÙvalidation for time series to ensure the validation and test errors are indeôÙ
pendent. The method is nonôÙdependent by not allowing any overlap of the observations in the
training, validation, and test set.
It is, therefore, possible to get unbiased estimates with the nonôÙdependent method, but leaveôÙoneôÙ
out validation was chosen for this project due to time and computational constraints. However, the
training, validation, and test splits apply the nonôÙdependent splits.
3.3
Training of Machine Learning Models on Multiple Time
Series
For machine learning models, training samples are made by striding over the time series as illusôÙ
trated in Figure 3.3. A time series with length N will have N ã(k + ü) samples for a model using
k past time steps to create multiôÙhorizon forecasts of size ü.
As described in Section 1.3.1, machine learning methods can be trained locally or globally on
multiple time series. For example, local and global methods can be applied if needed to forecast
the sale of beer in the next month in two stores. Each store has recorded a time series of earlier
beer sales. The first time series is length N and the second length M as presented in Figure 3.3.
The local method trains two models on each time series. On the contrary, the global model uses
a single model to forecast the beer sale for all stores. This means the global method has K + H
samples to learn from, while the local models have K and H samples, respectively.
Exploring Machine Learning Methods for Forecasting IoT Measurements in Wooden Structures

==PAGE:43==

3.4
Application of LightGBM for MultiôÙHorizon Forecasting
3.4.1
Implementation of Past and Future Variables
The LightGBM model trains on a matrix X with size N û M as input. By making the X maôÙ
trix consist of lagged and future values, the LightGBM can utilize past and future information to
create forecasts. This is illustrated in Figure 3.4 where observed inputs and target variables are
inserted into a row in X with lag k. For known future values, the values can also be inserted up
to forecasting horizon ü.
3.4.2
MultiôÙHorizon Forecasts
As mentioned in Section 1.3, multiôÙhorizon forecasts can be obtained using either the iterative or
direct method. Both methods can be applied to LightGBM. In the iterative method, the forecasts
are recursively reused as lagged variables in the next forecast. The direct method can be applied
by training h models to forecast h forecasting horizons. The two methods are illustrated in Figure
3.5.
It is chosen to apply the iterative method for multiôÙhorizon forecasting on the PM2.5 dataset as the
global method is too computationally costly. This will be further elaborated in Chapter 5. In the
explainability experiment, the methods are not relevant since only oneôÙstep forecasts are applied.

==PAGE:44==

...
...

Direct
Figure 3.5: The iterative and direct method for LightGBM.

3.5
Implementation of Models
This section explains how the hyperparameter optimization framework and each model is impleôÙ
mented.
The VAR model is implemented with the python statsmodels implementation1. The python packôÙ
age pmdarima2 is used for running AutoôÙARIMA [92] which uses the SARIMAX statsmodels imôÙ
plementation3.
The LightGBM model is implemented with the python package provided by Microsoft4. The LightôÙ
GBM python implementation is able to use quantile loss as loss function. The gradients of the loss
1VAR: ?iiTb,ffrrrXbiibKQ/2HbXQ`;fbi#H2fp2+iQ`n`X?iKH
2AutoôÙARIMA: ?iiT,ffHFHBM2@KHX+QKfTK/`BKf
3SARIMAX: ?iiTb,ffrrrXbiibKQ/2HbXQ`;f/2pf;2M2`i2/fbiibKQ/2HbXibXbii2bT+2Xb`BKtXa_AJsX?iKH
4LightGBM: https://lightgbm.readthedocs.io/en/v3.3.2/
Exploring Machine Learning Methods for Forecasting IoT Measurements in Wooden Structures
35
==PAGE:45==
will be either q or (1 ãq) depending on the residuals. However, the hessian then becomes zero
which causes unstable behavior in choosing splits and calculating wã.
LightGBM solves this by forcing the hessian to one in Equation 2.175 6 which makes it equivalent
to using firstôÙorder gradients to estimate best splits. However, setting the hessian to one does not
provide good approximations for wã. Therefore, the values of wãare estimated by calculating the
quantile of the observations in each leaf. The implementation has shown to achieve good results,
as mentioned in Section 1.3.3.
The Temporal Fusion Transformer is implemented using the python PyTorchôÙForecasting packôÙ
age7. The PyTorchôÙForecasting implementation automatically creates training samples as deôÙ
scribed in Section 3.3.
The PyTorchôÙForecasting application has made some changes to the original Temporal Fusion
Transformer. There is not any empirical evidence of these changes should provide a better model.
Therefore, the implementation is modified such that it is equal to the original implementation proôÙ
vided by Lim et al. [42]8.
However, the PyTorchôÙForecasting implementation automatically sets the embedding size which
is based on a rule of thumb formula by Jeremy Howard in a fast.ai lecture9. It was decided to
keep this automatic setting of the embedding size as it is based on empirical evidence and in
most cases results in less parameters than if the embedding size is set to hidden size (dmodel).
Additionally, the size of the embedding space is probably not critical because it is scaled in the
variable selection network to the hidden state size (dmodel).
Hyperparameter optimization is done using the TreeôÙStructured Parzen Estimator which is impleôÙ
mented with Optuna10. l(x) and g(x) are modelled by two Gaussian Mixture Models in the Optuna
implementation. The median stopping rule is used when training the Temporal Fusion Transformer
to speed up the tuning procedure. The median stopping rule keeps track of all intermediary valiôÙ
dation losses and stops training if an intermediary loss is above the median.

4
Explainability Evaluation on Synthetic
Dataset
The following section will describe the experiments done with synthetic datasets for evaluating
the interpretability correctness of the VAR, LightGBM, and TFT. The aim of the experiments is to
estimate which models are most likely to provide correct interpretability for forecasting moisture
values. To be able to evaluate interpretability correctness the models are evaluated on synthetic
datasets since it is necessary to know the true data generation mechanics.
Section 4.1 explains the synthetic dataset. Section 4.2 explains the applied methods and how the
interpretability correctness is measured. Explainability results are shown in Section 4.3. Finally,
the results are discussed in Section 4.4.
4.1
Synthetic dataset
The following section will describe how the synthetic datasets are generated and the properties of
each of the datasets.
The synthetic datasets originate from the paper Benchmarking AttentionôÙBased Interpretability of
Deep Learning in Multivariate Time Series Predictions [65]. The authors of the paper evaluate
different explainable attentionôÙbased deep learning methods on 10 multivariate synthetic datasets
with increasing complexity with respect to prediction performance and interpretability correctness.
To the extent of the knowledge of the authors of the paper, it is the only existing dataset to evaluate
interpretability correctness of explainable AI methods. It was chosen to use the synthetic datasets
from the paper as the paper establishes a baseline with respect to forecasting performance and
explainability which provides a background for evaluating the models in this experiment.
Due to time and computational constraints, 3 out of 10 datasets are evaluated, which are named
dataset 2, 6 and 8. The complexity of the datasets and hence challenge in providing correct
interpretability increases for each dataset. Moreover, the models will only be evaluated on one
realization of the synthetic dataset.
4.1.1
Dataset size and noise parameters
Each dataset consists of 20,000 observations, where dataset 2 and 6 have five features and
dataset 8 has four features. Each dataset has noise injected (üç) with probability f as can be
seen on Equation 4.1.
üçi =
2
N(ôç = ôçnoise, ü2 = ü2
noise),
p = f
0,
p = 1 ãf
(4.1)
The dataset parameters are set to the same as from the original paper, which are ôç = 0, ü = 0.1
and f = 0.3 [65]. The random number generator (seed) is also equal to the seed from the paper1.
4.1.2
Dataset 2
Dataset 2 consists of five autoregressive time series, Xi,t, with no interaction between the series
as can be seen on Equation 4.2 where i ã[0, 4]. The dataset is chosen to explore whether the
models are able to correctly identify the lags and independence between the five time series. If
the models fail at providing explanations that indicate independence between the variables, the
models are either overfitting or have weak interpretability capabilities. Due to the linearity of the
dataset, it should also be expected that the model is able to provide correct lags. Therefore, the
dataset functions as a baseline which the models must pass to be considered applicable.
1Github repository with data generation code: ?iiTb,ff;Bi?m#X+QKf?+@tBfKib@BMi2`T`2i#BHBiv@#2M+?K`F

4.1.3
Dataset 6

on X0 with varying lags as can be seen on Equation 4.3. Dataset 6 is chosen to explore whether
the models have the ability to identify the underlying source behind the data generation in a nonôÙ
linear dataset. This challenge is thought to be reminiscent of determining underlying factors in
the environment of wood driving the moisture values. The dataset also tests how well the models
are to generalize to different forecasting tasks as the lags are different for each variable. The
realization of the generative model can be seen in Figure 4.2.

4.2
Methods
The following section explains how the models are trained and optimized and how the forecasting
and interpretability performances are evaluated. The main focus in the experiments are evaluatôÙ
ing the interpretability correctness. However, the forecasting performance of the models is also
evaluated to understand if the models are fitted poorly to the data which can potentially impact
interpretability.
4.2.1
Training, Validation and Test splits
To make an unbiased evaluation of the forecasting and interpretability performance, a holdôÙout
test set is made for each dataset which is the last 2,000 observations of each of the datasets. For
determining the hyperparameters of the LightGBM and TFT model, a holdôÙout validation set is set
aside, which corresponds to 10% of the remaining training set resulting in 1,800 observations.
4.2.2
Model Training and Selection
This section will describe the procedure in training and selecting a set of hyperparameters for the
LightGBM and TFT to evaluate interpretability correctness on the test set.
Exploring Machine Learning Methods for Forecasting IoT Measurements in Wooden Structures
39
==PAGE:49==
It is a prerequisite that the models are fitted properly to the data so that they can provide correct
interpretation since an overfitted model will learn random and wrong dependencies while an unôÙ
derfitted model will fail in learning the dependencies properly. Therefore, it is necessary to find a
good set of hyperparameters for each model. However, since the main focus of this experiment
is evaluating interpretability correctness, it is assumed that it is not necessary to find the optimal
hyperparameters. It is assumed that a model with good hyperparameters, is fitted sufficiently well
to the data to provide good interpretability. Therefore, the focus of the hyperparameter search is
finding a good set of parameters, which is reflected in some of the hyperparameter ranges.
All models are trained to make oneôÙstep forecasts since making longer steps will not give more
information regarding the ability to provide correct interpretability. All models have a lookôÙback
window of 10 steps.
Vector Autoregressive Model Training
The VAR model has no hyperparameters to tune because p it preset to 10. Therefore, it is fitted
directly to the training data. For comparison on the test set, the VAR model is trained on the
combined training and validation set.
LightGBM Training
As mentioned, the LightGBM model is limited by the fact that it only provides oneôÙdimensional
output which means that three models are trained to provide 0.1, 0.5, and 0.9 quantiles.
The LightGBM model is trained with a local and global training method. The local method involves
training a model to provide forecasts for M variables which results in a total of 3 ôñ M trained LightôÙ
GBM models per synthetic dataset. The global method trains a single model to provide forecasts
for each variable by providing the model with a categorical variable denoting, which variable it
should forecast. Therefore, the global method will have a total of three models per dataset. This
means the global method has M times more data to train on.
In the case of forecasting moisture values, the global method method is preferable since WoodôÙ
sense prefers a single model for handling all forecasting. This is due to economic and time benefits
in terms storing the forecasts and training the models.
Both the local and global method are tested in evaluating interpretability performance as it is inôÙ
teresting to explore whether the global method is able to generalize to different forecasting tasks
concerning explainability.
There is no future known inputs in the synthetic dataset. Therefore, all variables are lagged with
lag 10 as described in Section 3.4.1.
The LightGBM model has 10 hyperparameters that are are tuned by 200 hyperparameter trials. As
described in Section 3.4.3, the three LightGBM models has hyperparameters optimized jointly with
the mean quantile loss QLmean. Details on hyperparameter intervals, optimal hyperparameters
and training and validation errors for best model on training and validation set can be found in
Appendix D.1.
Temporal Fusion Transformer Training
The TFT is trained to be forecast all the variables. A static categorical variable is used to give the
model information on which target variable it should forecast. Since the static variables are used
to condition the TFT in variable selection and initial hidden state weights in the LSTM, the TFT
should be able to learn the different dependencies per variable it is predicting. All inputs and the
target variable are standardized to a normal distribution.
The TFT has five hyperparameters to tune. The TFT is a big model, therefore, the choice of
hyperparameters reflects a focus on avoiding the TFT to overfit. Due to the larger computational
cost of training the TFT, the hyperparameter intervals are narrowed to intervals with smaller risk
of overfitting. The hyperparameters are optimized with 40 trials.
The models are trained with maximum 20 epochs. Here, early stopping is set to patience of four
and minimum validation loss improvement of 0.0005. Moreover, it was chosen to evaluate the
validation loss every 1
2 epoch due to the model trains in a few epochs and it is possible the optimal
model is between epochs.
40
Exploring Machine Learning Methods for Forecasting IoT Measurements in Wooden Structures
==PAGE:50==
Thus, if the validation loss does not improve for two epochs, the training is stopped. Learning rate
reduction is also applied by halving the learning rate when the validation loss at the end of each
epoch does not improve.
There is not any future known observations available for the TFT model. Therefore, the decoder
part of the network is provided a constant value as the network otherwise would not work. The lag
(k) is set to 10 such that the 10 past values of each variable is passed to the encoder.
The hyperparameter intervals, optimal hyperparameters, and training and validation loss per epoch
can be found in Appendix D.2.
During interpretability evaluation of the TFT, it was observed that the attention weight of the TFT
was either uniform or focused on a single timestep. It was hypothesized the poor explainability
was due to the two skip connections that are passing the attention layer which allows the model
to not utilize attention (see Figure 2.12).
To test if this is the case, a TFT model without the two skip connections passing the attention
layer is also trained with hyperparameter optimization and tested with respect to explainability. By
removing the two skip connections, the model is more likely to utilize attention. Additionally, two
other variations was also shortly explored on dataset 2. The first variant is a TFT where only the
skip connection to the final layer is removed (purple striped line in Figure 2.12). The second variant
is a TFT model where positional encoding is substituting the LSTM layer. The three models are
respectively named TFTôÙnoôÙskip, TFTôÙoneôÙskip and TFTôÙposôÙenc. The TFT model with both skip
connections is named TFTôÙwôÙskip.
The TFTôÙoneôÙskip model is used to explore if it is the skip connection from the LSTM layer directly
to the last layer that causes attention not to be uniform. The TFTôÙposôÙenc explores if it is the
ãconnectionã between timesteps in the LSTM layer that causes the uniform attention. These two
models are both tested on dataset 2 because the attention is very uniform for TFTôÙwôÙskip on this
dataset and the models should minimum perform well on dataset 2 to be explored on the other
datasets.
The TFTôÙoneôÙskip and TFTôÙposôÙenc model uses the optimal hyperparameters from the TFTôÙnoôÙ
skip and TFTôÙwôÙskip models, respectively because of time constraints. It would, of course, be
better it they had hyperparameter optimization conducted on their own since it can be ruled out if
poor explainability is due to wrong hyperparameters.
It was also observed the interpretability correctness of a TFT model with the same hyperparamôÙ
eters varies per training. Therefore, the TFT models are trained five times. The forecasting and
explainability is evaluated with respect to the mean performance of the five trained models.
4.2.3
Forecasting and Explainability Evaluation
The forecasting and explainability performances are respectively evaluated quantitatively and
qualitatively.
To evaluate forecasting performance, the models are evaluated with respect to mean square erôÙ
ror (MSE), MAE, and 0.1 and 0.9 quantile loss (QL0.1 and QL0.9). The 0.5 quantile loss is not
reported because 1
2MAE = QL0.5. MSE is reported instead of RMSE since the paper with the
synthetic dataset evaluates the models on MSE. Additionally, the MSE and MAE error from a naive
forecasting model is also calculated to establish a baseline for the models.
The qualitative evaluation of the explainability correctness is based on the paper from which the
data originates where the interpretability correctness is evaluated with respect to öÝ and öý matrices
[65]. The öÝ coefficients of the matrix describe the ratio of dependence that a variable has on all
the other lagged variables including itself. The öÝ matrices will have size M û10 where M denotes
the number of variables and 10 refers to the number of lags. Since the öÝ coefficients denote the
ratio of dependence, the coefficients sum to one.
The öý matrix describes the ratio of dependence a variable Xi has on Xj, where i is the row index
and j is the column index which means the öý matrix has size M ûM, where the coefficients of each
row will summarize to one. It should be noted that authors denote the öÝ and öý coefficients as autoôÙ
correlation and crossôÙcorrelation matrices, however, since they are nonôÙnegative and summarize
Exploring Machine Learning Methods for Forecasting IoT Measurements in Wooden Structures
41
==PAGE:51==
to one, this is arguably a wrong interpretation.
Examples of ground truth matrices for each dataset is illustrated in Figure 4.4, 4.5 and 4.6. öÝ
coefficients are shown with blue heatmaps and öý coefficients are shown with green heatmaps.
The examples for ground truth variables are based on the coefficients of X0 in Equation 4.2,
coefficients of X4 in Equation 4.3 and coefficients of X2 in Equation 4.4.

4.3
Results
In the following section, the results of the explainability evaluation are shown. Due to the primary
focus of this experiment being to evaluate explainability correctness, the results in forecasting
performance are moved to Appendix D.3.
To summarize the results, the appendix shows that the machine learning models are comparable
in forecasting performance to the models in the original paper by Baric et al. [65]. Compared
to LightGBMôÙlocal, the LightGBMôÙglobal model has a higher error in all metrics on all datasets,
which points towards it struggling to generalize to different forecasting tasks. On the other hand,
the TFTôÙnoôÙskip and TFTôÙwôÙskip have similar performances.
VAR significantly outperforms the other models on dataset 2, which is not surprising since all
the model assumptions apply to dataset 2. In dataset 6, the TFT models mostly perform best
concerning MSE and quantile loss. In dataset 8, LightGBMôÙlocal is significantly better than the
other models. The TFT models also perform well as it significantly outperforms the models used
in the original paper. VAR still performs well compared to the other models even though it cannot
fit its predictions to change in variable behavior. It is shown in Appendix D.5 that the VAR struggles
to adjust its forecasts at times the behavior of the variables changes.
4.3.1
Interpretability Correctness Evaluation
The following section will review the ability of the models to provide correct interpretability with
respect to the öÝ and öý coefficient plots.
Dataset 2
In dataset 2, the models are only reviewed with respect to öÝ coefficients, since it is of interest to
determine if the models identify the correct lag. In Appendix D.7, the öÝ and öý coefficients for all
models in dataset 2 are shown.
The öÝ coefficients for the VAR model are illustrated in Figure 4.7. The VAR model correctly idenôÙ
tifies that all variables are independent of each other and also correctly identifies the correct lag
and it approximates correct ratio of dependence, which is expected since the model assumptions
of linear autoregressive behavior is equivalent to dataset 2.

4.4
Discussion
The following section will first discuss the results of the interpretability study. Afterward, the perôÙ
formance of the models is put into perspective with respect to interpretability performance on the
Woodsense dataset. Finally, the limitations of the experiments will be discussed.
4.4.1
Interpretability Discussion
The LightGBMôÙglobal model does not benefit from training on multiple variables since it is inferior in
both forecasting and explainability performance to the LightGBMôÙlocal model. That is even though
it is trained with more data, and is conditioned to what variable it should forecast. This indicates
that the LightGBMôÙglobal model also can struggle on forecasting on the Woodsense data, where it
should learn to generalize to different sensors in different settings. On the other hand, LightGBMôÙ
local does arguably provide best interpretability, since it is among the best in both dataset 2 and
6 and is second to TFTôÙwôÙskip in dataset 8. This indicates that SHAP is a good tool to achieve
correct interpretability when applied on treeôÙbased models.
The TFTôÙoneôÙskip model did fail to provide correct lags on dataset 2 even though it did not have
uniform attention. The reason for this is unclear. It can potentially be due to the skipôÙconnection
passing the attention layer leads to the attention layer controls what information from the LSTM
cell that should be suppressed. However, what information should be suppressed at given time
steps does not necessarily align with the true lags.
The TFTôÙposôÙenc model has uniform attention which is surprising as there is no part of the model
(except for the attention layer) that connects the inputs at various time steps. However, this further
indicates that it is the skip connections that can result in uniform attention.
During evaluation, it was noted that the TFTôÙnoôÙskip model performs better variable selection than
the TFTôÙwôÙskip model as can be seen in Appendix D.7, D.8, and D.9. It was initially believed the
opposite would be the case, because skipôÙconnections would allow easier flow of the gradients to
the Variable Selection Network. It might be due to the fact that the attention layer helps focus the
flow of gradients to the correct timestep where a variable is important. However, this is something
that would be interesting to explore further.
52
Exploring Machine Learning Methods for Forecasting IoT Measurements in Wooden Structures
==PAGE:62==
Neither LightGBMôÙlocal nor TFTôÙnoôÙskip did well in providing correct explainability when X0,tã5 ãÊ
0.5. This is possibly due to there is more observations where X0,tã5 > 0.5, which makes the
models put more weight on X0.
4.4.2
Woodsense dataset
If the decision of choosing which model to use to forecast moisture were based solely on the inôÙ
terpretability, then the decision would be on the LightGBMôÙlocal model because it, as mentioned
overall, provides the best explainability. However, using the LightGBMôÙlocal model has some
drawbacks because it would mean a LightGBM model will be trained on relatively few samples
(on average 1,000 per sensor) and thus will not be able to learn from global tendencies. ConsidôÙ
ering that the TFT model is globally trained and can adapt its forecasts to different variables, it
makes it a serious contender to be trained on the Woodsense data. However, the longôÙterm foreôÙ
casting performance of moisture values is weighted higher than the ability to attribute with good
explainability. Therefore, the model choice depends on the forecasting performance of the models
on the PM2.5 dataset.
4.4.3
Limitations
The experiment has a number of limitations which should be taken into consideration. First of all
are the models only tested on one realization of the generative models which means the concluôÙ
sions of the forecasting and interpretability performance of the models are limited to the realization
of the dataset and not to the generative models themselves. Furthermore, due to CV not being
performed, it is not possible to estimate the model uncertainty with respect to how well a model
generalizes on different datasets.
Evidently, the synthetic datasets are not the same as the Woodsense dataset and therefore are
the explainability performance of the models not guaranteed to be the same on other datasets.
Arguably, it would have been better to design a synthetic dataset which emulates the Woodsense
dataset. However, it was assessed the impact of the environment on the moisture is not well
enough understood to a synthetic dataset would be close enough to the Woodsense dataset.
Furthermore, it would have been time costly to develop such dataset.
Finally, it was realized late in the process that the evaluation of interpretability correctness could
have been done quantitatively. For instance, the öÝ and öý ground truth matrices could have been
subtracted with the estimated matrices and then calculated a mean absolute error. This would
have made the evaluation process more succinct and less biased.

5
Forecasting Evaluation on PM2.5
Dataset
The following section will describe the experiment for evaluating multiôÙhorizon forecasting perforôÙ
mance on the PM2.5 Beijing airôÙquality dataset1 of SARIMAX, LightGBM, and TFT. The experiôÙ
ment aims to determine which model should be used for forecasting moisture content values in
the Woodsense dataset.
Specifically, the models are evaluated on their ability to forecast PM2.5 with a 24ôÙhour horizon.
The PM2.5 dataset is chosen due to its similarities with the Woodsense data and the availability of
literature to compare the models. The PM2.5 dataset consists of air measurements and weather
data. The task is to forecast PM2.5 levels dependent on the physical and chemical interaction with
the other weather features.
The weather data is assumed to be available in the future such that the modeling task is equivalent
to the Woodsense moisture forecasting task. However, the dataset only contains a single long time
series, whereas the Woodsense data consists of multiple time series from different sensors that
can behave differently.
Section 5.1 explains the PM2.5 dataset. Section 5.2 describes the training and hyperparameter
optimization methodology for the three models. Afterward in Section 5.3, the forecasting perforôÙ
mance of the models will be presented. Finally, Section 5.4 will provide a discussion concerning
the choice of model for forecasting moisture content concerning explainability and forecasting perôÙ
formance.
5.1
PM2.5 Dataset
The PM2.5 dataset is originally from the paper ãAssessing Beijingãs PM2.5 pollution: severity,
weather impact, APEC and winter heatingã by Liang et al. [93] where the authors evaluate the
severity of air quality in Beijing and how different features impact the air quality. The PM2.5 dataset
in total consists of hourly observations from 2021ôÙ01ôÙ01 to 2014ôÙ12ôÙ31 resulting in 43,824 obserôÙ
vations. The PM2.5 levels through the years can be seen in Figure 5.1. The figure shows that the
PM2.5 levels are higher during the winter and lower during the summer. The dataset has a total
of 12 features, including PM2.5 measurements, as seen in Table 5.1.
Figure 5.1: Plot of PM2.5 levels (ôçg/m3) from 2021ôÙ01ôÙ01 to 2014ôÙ12ôÙ31 in Beijing.
The dew point denotes the required temperature (ô¯C) for the water in the air to condense, where
a higher amount of water in the air will lead to a higher dew point. Combined wind direction
denotes the dominating wind direction from the past hour where there are four dominating wind
directions which are ãNorthôÙEastã (NE), ãNorthôÙWestã (NW), ãSouthôÙEastã (SE), and ãCalm and

The authors of the dataset found that PM2.5 is highly influenced by meteorological conditions
where dew point and pressure are most influential on PM2.5 levels, followed by temperature and
accumulated wind direction. Rain and snow are significant in summer and winter, respectively.
Appendix E.1 provides a more comprehensive analysis of correlation and trends in the dataset.
5.1.1
Preprocessing and Dataset Splits
The dataset contains approximately 5% PM2.5 missing values. In Appendix E.2, can be seen a
figure of where missing values are distributed through the dataset. The missing values are forward
filled with the latest, not missing value. However, the first 24 PM2.5 values are missing and cannot
be imputed by forward filling. Thus, the first 24 observations are removed.
The validation and test set contain a full year of data to ensure the models are evaluated on all the
seasons since the seasons have varying distribution and temporal behavior of the PM2.5 levels.
The exact splits can be seen on Table 5.2.

5.2
Methods
The following section will explain the modeling choices and hyperparameter optimization for each
model. As mentioned, the aim of evaluating the forecasting performance of the models on the
PM2.5 dataset is to estimate what model will perform best on the Woodsense dataset. Therefore,
the modeling choices are based on what would have been reasonable if training on the Woodsense
dataset.
5.2.1
SARIMAX
As mentioned in Chapter 3, it is decided to use AutoôÙARIMA to find the optimal model order. AutoôÙ
ARIMA is used because it would have been used in the setting of Woodsense, where there are
351 sensors. It would be costly in time and labor to continuously find the optimal model order for
each of these sensors.
Data transformations and feature engineering are done before using AutoôÙARIMA. The exogeôÙ
nous features in the SARIMAX model are ãmonthã, ãhourã, ãdew pointã, ãtemperatureã, ãpressureã,
56
Exploring Machine Learning Methods for Forecasting IoT Measurements in Wooden Structures
==PAGE:66==
ãwind speedã, ãsnowfallã, ãrainfallã, and ãwind directionã. The ãmonthã, ãhourã, and ãwind directionã
features are oneôÙhot encoded.
The PM2.5 data is also transformed such that yt = log (PM2.5 + 1) because the data is heavily
rightôÙskewed as can be seen on the left plot on Figure 5.2. The data becomes closer to being
normally distributed after the transformation, as seen on the right plot. One is added before log
transformation because there are a few 0 values in the data.
Figure 5.2: Distribution of PM2.5 and log(PM2.5 + 1).
Figure 5.3: Normalized residuals after fitting of linear regression model with exogenous variables
to PM2.5 and log(PM2.5 + 1). The left plot shows the normalized residuals while the right plot
shows the residuals differentiated (i.e. rest ãrestã1). The plot shows that the variance of PM2.5
residuals does not become constant after differentiation.
SARIMAX models assume the data to be wideôÙsense stationery where one condition is the variôÙ
ance to be constant through time. Figure 5.3 shows the normalized residuals with different transôÙ
formations. The variance appears to become constant when the residuals of log(PM2.5 + 1) is
differenced. The other transformations does not appear to achieve constant variance.
Finally, the features wind direction, rainfall, and snowfall are also logôÙtransformed because it
makes the relationship to log(PM2.5 + 1) close to linear, which can be seen in Appendix E.3.
The SARIMAX model has been trained with two approaches. The first approach performs AutoôÙ
ARIMA on the last year of the training set from 2012/01ôÙ2012/12 to determine the optimal modeling
order. It was discovered that it was very timeôÙconsuming and memory intensive to train on all the
training data and led to memory problems on the computer when the accumulated orders of the
model got too high. Additionally, it is unlikely the model would benefit much more from training on
more data due to SARIMAX models usually being used and designed for data in lower quantities.
The optimal model found in AutoôÙARIMA is tested on the whole validation set. Later, the model is
retrained on the validation set for forecasting evaluation on the test set.
Exploring Machine Learning Methods for Forecasting IoT Measurements in Wooden Structures
57
==PAGE:67==
In the second approach, AutoôÙARIMA is performed each month in the validation dataset and is
then tested on the following month. This means an optimal SARIMAX model is found for each
month. The second approach is added because it reflects how the models would be trained in a
Woodsense setting where a model would be continuously fitted to the latest data to make forecasts.
The two approaches are illustrated in Figure 5.4.
The models from the two approaches will respectively be named SARIMAXôÙyear and SARIMAXôÙ
month.

In Appendix E.4 are the initialization parameters for AutoôÙARIMA shown. Parameters controlling
the maximum allowed order of seasonality (Q and P) are based on autocorrelation and partial
autocorrelation plots shown in Appendix E.5. The optimal found model for SARIMAXôÙyear and
SARIMAXôÙmonth can be seen in Appendix E.6.
5.2.2
LightGBM
It needed to be considered if the LightGBM model should provide forecasts with an iterative or
direct approach. The iterative and direct approach for LightGBM is explained in Section 3.4.2. It
was decided to use the iterative method because the direct method would be too computationally
expensive to train. As described in Section 3.4.3, it is needed to train three models to provide
0.1, 0.5 and 0.9 quantile forecasts. This means a total of 3 û 24 = 72 models would be trained,
which approximately takes 18 minutes to train. With 300 trials, it will take 3.75 days to optimize
the hyperparameter on the PM2.5 dataset with 43,824 observations. The Woodsense dataset has
around 300,000 observations after preprocessing and will have a forecasting horizon of 84 steps
(equivalent to 14 days), making hyperparameter optimization infeasible for the direct method.
Two variants of the LightGBM model are tested, a model only using lagged variables and one
which additionally uses feature engineering on each of the features. Feature engineering can
improve performance as treeôÙbased models do not have the capabilities of a neural network to
extract feature representations. The two variations will be named LightGBMôÙlag and LightGBMôÙ
feat. The reason for training two models is that LightGBMôÙlag arguably is easier to interpret beôÙ
cause its feature importances using SHAP are related to the observations only. At the same time,
LightGBMôÙfeat is tested to see if it will improve to such an extent that it is preferable to lose some
interpretability.
The features used for training LightGBM are the same as for SARIMAX, except that PM2.5 is
included as input. The features can be divided into observed and known future inputs. As menôÙ
tioned, it is assumed that correct forecast information is available and therefore are, the features
dew point, temperature, pressure, wind speed, snowfall, and rainfall used as future known inputs.
Month and hour are also used as future known inputs because they are also known in the future.
58

GBM in behaving differently at lower or higher mean values.
ãÂ Median: The median of all lagged features. It can also provide contextual information.
ãÂ Standard deviation: Standard deviation of all lagged features. It can also provide contexôÙ
tual information to determine the quantiles, for instance.
ãÂ Quantiles: The 0.05, 0.1, 0.25, 0.75, 0.9 and 0.95 quantiles.
ãÂ Maximum and minimum value: The max and minimum of each lagged variable feature. It
can also provide extra contextual information and enrich understanding of mean and stanôÙ
dard deviation measures.
ãÂ Count above and below mean: The number of observations above and below the mean,
respectively. Can give contextual information on how to interpret the mean value.
ãÂ Mean change: Defined as
1

t xt+1 ãxt. If positive, the values of the time series have
increased and vice versa. Can give contextual information regarding if the values lately are
increasing or decreasing.
ãÂ Absolute mean change: Defined as

the values on the average change which can provide contextual information on how stable
the PM2.5 values are.
ãÂ Maximum and minimum location: Gives the index of the maximum and minimum values.
Can give contextual information of how long ago the latest peak was. For example, it can give
LightGBM information on whether the PM2.5 levels are currently increasing or decreasing.
ãÂ Number of peaks: Counts how many peaks there are for each lagged feature. A peak is
defined as the highest value among the five surrounding timesteps.
The hyperparameters of the three LightGBM models are again tuned jointly with the mean quanôÙ
tile 0.1, 0.5, and 0.9 loss. It was chosen to evaluate the models concerning oneôÙstep forecasts
assuming that the optimal model for oneôÙstep forecasts also will be the optimal model for multiôÙ
horizon forecasts. The computational cost of evaluating the forecasting capabilities of each model
Exploring Machine Learning Methods for Forecasting IoT Measurements in Wooden Structures
59
==PAGE:69==
on 24ôÙhour horizons takes approximately 2 minutes and 30 seconds for each model. Training
LightGBMôÙlag and LightGBMôÙfeat take 2 minutes and 6 seconds and 2 minutes and 40 seconds,
respectively. This means it will take 22 and 23.5 hours to optimize the hyperparameters for each
model, which is feasible. However, it was estimated that it would take too much time to use the
same hyperparameter optimization on the Woodsense dataset. However, it is likely that the best
model for oneôÙstep forecasts also will be the best at multiôÙhorizon forecasting since a model with
the lowest oneôÙstep errors will have lower error accumulation when predicting at multiôÙhorizon
steps.
Due to forecasting performance being in focus, the hyperparameter search range was expanded
to a broader range than in the explainability section. The provided search range for the hyperpaôÙ
rameter search is provided in Appendix E.7. Lags are added to the hyperparameters because it is
challenging to determine how much prior information the models would need. The hyperparameter
optimization search results can be seen in Appendix E.8.
After the optimal hyperparameters are identified, the models are retrained five times with the opôÙ
timal hyperparameters on the training set and five times on the combined training and validation
set for obtaining standard deviation for the validation and test set to estimate training uncertainty.
5.2.3
Temporal Fusion Transformer
TFTôÙwôÙskip and TFTôÙnoôÙskip presented in Chapter 4 are also evaluated on the PM2.5 dataset.
The encoder and decoder input to the model is the same, except that PM2.5 is not given as input
to the decoder. The static encoder is not provided any inputs because the model is trained on a
single time series. Each feature and target is standardized with respect to the mean and standard
deviation of each feature in the training set.
Feature transformations is applied to the ãmonthã and ãhourã feature to make it easier for the model
to interpret the features. The ãhourã and ãmonthã features are projected to the unit circle using both
sinus and cosinus functions, as can be seen in Equation 5.1 where x is either the month or hour
value and period is either 12 months or 24 hours. The transformations should make the model
understand there is the exact difference between 12:00ôÙ13:00 and 23:00ôÙ00:00. The reason for
using cosinus and sinus functions is the unique month or hour value can be identified on the unit
circle. Thus, the two features become four features which are named ãhour_sinã, ãhour_cosã,
ãmonth_sinã, and ãmonth_cosã.
featuresin = sin
! x2ü
period
"
featurecos = cos
! x2ü
period
"
(5.1)
Additionally, early stopping is used with patience of six and a minimum validation loss improvement
of 0.1. The validation loss is checked every 1
2 epoch. Thus, the training is stopped if the validation
loss does not improve for three epochs. Learning rate decay is also applied by halving the learning
rate when the validation loss at the end of each epoch does not improve. During hyperparameter
optimization, the maximum number of allowed epochs is set to 20. The model is trained with the
Adam optimizer.
The hyperparameters are tuned with 50 trials. The hyperparameter range is set according to the
range used in the original paper [42]. However, hyperparameter ranges for the number of LSTM
layers and lags are made broader. The hyperparameter search range can be seen in Appendix
E.9 and the optimal set of hyperparameters can be seen in Appendix E.10.
To estimate training uncertainty, the model with optimal hyperparameters is retrained five times
on the training set to calculate the standard deviation of evaluation metrics on the validation set.
Afterward, the model with the same optimal hyperparameters is trained on the combined training
and validation set five times to estimate the forecasting performance and training uncertainty on
the test set.

Exploring Machine Learning Methods for Forecasting IoT Measurements in Wooden Structures
==PAGE:70==
5.2.4
Evaluation Metrics
The following section will explain the applied metrics to evaluate forecasting performance. The
probabilistic forecasts are evaluated with 0.1 and 0.9 quantile loss which measures how well the
model respectively provide lower and upper prediction intervals. Both RMSE and MAE is used to
evaluate the forecasts. RMSE penalizes high errors more due to the errors are squared. ThereôÙ
fore, the RMSE can give indications of if a model has very high errors or errors with high variance.
MAE, on the other hand, gives an indication of the average absolute error which gives an indication
of what the average error is of the model.
5.3
Results
In Table 5.3, the RMSE, MAE, QL0.1, and QL0.9 are reported with one standard deviation. The
evaluation is done with 24ôÙhour forecasting steps and a stride of six. Thus, the forecasts are
evaluated for t, t + 6, ..., t + n. A stride of six was selected as the evaluation otherwise took a long
time for the SARIMAX models that need to be updated to provide a new forecast.
The SARIMAX models do not have standard deviation because the AR and MA coefficients in the
Statsmodels python package are initialized using ConditionalôÙsumôÙofôÙsquares [69]. It was tested
by initializing the model with 0 weights, but the model failed to compute. Therefore, the models
are reported with 0 standard deviations. The training and validation errors of the five LightGBM
and TFT models trained with optimal hyperparameters can be seen in Appendix E.11 and E.12.

TFTôÙwôÙskip is due to the TFTôÙnoôÙskip having higher errors in the first forecasting steps than the

and MAE during the higher forecasting horizons. However, the two LightGBM models struggle to
provide correct 0.1 quantiles where they have much higher errors than the other models where
LightGBMôÙlag especially has high QL0.1 errors. Likewise, the SARIMAXôÙmonth model has a much
higher QL0.9 loss than the other models, where the SARIMAXôÙyear model has a much lower loss.
5.3.1
Discussion of Validation Results
The TFT models perform better than all the other models on all metrics except for the first forecastôÙ
ing horizons. If the aim were to provide shorterôÙterm forecasts up to around five steps, then the
Exploring Machine Learning Methods for Forecasting IoT Measurements in Wooden Structures
61
==PAGE:71==
Figure 5.6: Validation set errors for each model per forecasting horizon.
LightGBMôÙfeat model would be the preferred model. However, in the case of Woodsense, having
correct longôÙterm forecasts would be more valuable because the customers can trust the model
to plan longôÙterm.
However, it is interesting that both TFT models have higher RMSE and MAE compared to the other
models in the first forecasting steps. It might be due to the models are optimized on all forecasting
steps simultaneously and therefore trades higher shortôÙterm forecasting performance for better
longôÙterm forecasting performance. The difference in the performance of the first forecasting steps
between the two TFT models is probably because the TFTôÙwôÙskip model can directly use the recent
information processed by the LSTM layer.
The higher QL0.1 losses for the two LightGBM models are likely due to the models struggle to
provide quantiles close to 0 or at 0 if the 0.1 quantile of the observations in the leaf nodes has
higher values.
The SARIMAXôÙmonth model likely has a high QL0.9 loss because its prediction intervals are based
on earlier errors. If it is trained on a month with lower PM2.5 values it will underestimate the 0.9
quantiles in the following month with higher PM2.5 values.
5.3.2
Test Set Forecasting Performance

performance of the models is almost similar, except that TFTôÙnoôÙskip has slightly lower RMSE loss
than TFTôÙwôÙskip, and SARIMAXôÙmonth has lower RMSE and MAE errors than SARIMAXôÙyear.
The performance gap between the LightGBMôÙfeat and TFTôÙnoôÙskip model has become greater on
RMSE and MAE. However, the SARIMAXôÙmonth stills struggle with giving good QL0.9 forecasts.
The errors on the test set are lower for all models compared to the validation set. This is likely
due to the test set being easier to forecast because it does not have as many spikes in PM2.5

with an RMSE of 65.35 [11]. Two other papers make forecasting horizons up to respectively 6 and

5.4
Discussion and Choice of Model for Moisture Content
Forecasting
The following section will first discuss the forecasting performance and put it into perspective in
choosing the final model for forecasting moisture content. Thus, this discussion will motivate the
choice of model for moisture content forecasting concerning both explainability and forecasting
performance. Finally, the limitations of the forecasting performance experiment will be discussed.
5.4.1
Test Set Results Discussion
The performance of the models on the validation and test sets are very similar. Therefore, the
points in the discussion for the validation set also apply to the test set. Except, the forecasting
errors of the TFTôÙwôÙskip are significantly lower in the first steps when compared to the errors of
the other models. This could be due to the TFTôÙwôÙskip having trained on more data. However,
the TFTôÙnoôÙskip still struggles with higher errors on the first steps. It points towards that recent
information from the LSTM still becomes lost due to no skipôÙconnections.
Both TFT architectures outperform the other models concerning 24ôÙhour forecasts. The error of
the forecasts increases less than in the other models. This is probably due they do not suffer from
error accumulation as the other models that use the iterative method. The TFT models also have
a lower loss than the other models on all four metrics, showing they perform well overall.
5.4.2
Choice of Model for Development on Woodsense Data
If the model choice should be solely based on the PM2.5 dataset, then the choice would fall upon
TFTôÙwôÙskip as it overall performs the best.
Concerning explainability, it was found that LightGBMôÙlocal performed the best. Using the local
method would mean a LightGBM model should be fitted to each sensor. However, training a
model on each sensor would be computationally costly and have relatively few samples to train
on. The other alternative is to train a global LightGBM model. However, it was concluded in
the explainability experiment that the LightGBMôÙglobal model struggled to fit different forecasting
tasks.
Luckily, the TFTôÙnoôÙskip provides relatively good explainability and multiôÙhorizon forecasts. As
mentioned, forecasting performance is weighted higher than explainability correctness. The TFTôÙ
noôÙskip performs particularly well in the forecasting experiments where only TFTôÙwôÙskip is betôÙ
ter. However, the TFTôÙwôÙskip provided very poor explainability in the explainability experiments.
Therefore, TFTôÙnoôÙskip is chosen as the model to be developed to provide explainable moisture
content forecasts.
5.4.3
Limitations
The forecasting horizon is only 24ôÙhours for the PM2.5 dataset. On the contrary, the forecasting
horizon for moisture content is 14 days. Therefore, it cannot be concluded that the TFT will perform
better than the other iterative models on longer forecasting horizons. However, it is a reasonable
assumption since it is likely that the error accumulation in the iterative methods will continue to
grow.
The choice of forecasting horizon was a tradeôÙoff between evaluation time and having a long
enough forecasting horizon to put results into perspective to the Woodsense data. Evaluation
time was especially a problem for the SARIMAX models that took approximately 24ôÙ48 hours to
evaluate on the validation and test sets. This is because the model needed to be updated each
time a new forecast should be made. The LightGBM models took about an hour to evaluate as
the forecasts were made iteratively. On the contrary, the TFT models provided swift evaluation
and only took about a minute. Therefore, the TFT model is preferred considering inference costs.
64
Exploring Machine Learning Methods for Forecasting IoT Measurements in Wooden Structures
==PAGE:74==
Another obvious limitation is that it is not given that the performance of the models is similar to the
Woodsense data as the datasets obviously are different. Also, PM2.5 has a single long multivariate
time series where the data of Woodsense has multiple shorter multivariate time series with varying
mean and variance.
It would also have been nice to analyze if the provided explainability of TFTôÙnoôÙskip and TFTôÙwôÙskip
also is different in the PM2.5 dataset. It would be more challenging to determine the correctness
of the explainability, but it would probably be possible to get a general idea of the explainability is
reasonable. However, this was not done due to time constraints.

==PAGE:75==

Exploring Machine Learning Methods for Forecasting IoT Measurements in Wooden Structures
==PAGE:76==
6
Moisture Content Forecasting on
Woodsense Data
This chapter will describe how the TFT model is developed and tuned to forecast moisture content
with a horizon of 14 days.
The development of the final model consists of feature selection to find a good set of features
that lead to improvements in forecasting. If the features do not improve the model, it is assumed
they lead to overfitting or do not have additional useful information. Thus, the interpretability conôÙ
cerning these features cannot be trusted. Feature selection is based on evaluating the models
on a validation set. During training, it is assumed that correct forecasts are available, i.e., historic
weather features are used for training. The model is trained on the historic weather data as there
is more data available. Training on the forecasted weather data will probably also introduce a lot
of noise that will result in an inferior model.
The best model from feature selection is evaluated on a validation set with weather forecasts as
input. This is to assess if the forecasts still are acceptable and if any adjustments are necessary
before the model is trained on the combined training and validation data and tested on a holdôÙout
test set. The forecasting performance with historic and forecasted weather data is evaluated on
the test set.
Afterward, specific forecasting examples are chosen to analyze how the TFT can be applied conôÙ
cerning its forecasts and explainability capabilities. Finally, the feasibility of Woodsense applying
the TFT to forecast moisture content is discussed.
The structure of this chapter is the following. Section 6.1 will explain the Woodsense data and how
it is preprocessed before training. Section 6.2 will explain the applied methods in feature selection
and model training. In Section 6.3, the validation set results for feature selection and final model
performance with historic and forecasted weather data are shown. The test set results of the
final model with historic and forecasted weather data as inputs are shown in Section 6.4. Then
forecasting examples are analysed in Section 6.5. Finally, the results and feasibility of applying
the TFT in production are discussed in Section 6.6.
6.1
Woodsense Data
6.1.1
Sensor Data of Woody
The following section will shortly explain the sensor of Woodsense. The main aim of the sensor
is to monitor the moisture content, which is measured in % weight of water with respect to the dry
weight as can be on Equation 6.1 [7].
Moisture content = (kg wet wood ãkg dry wood)
kg dry wood
ôñ 100%
(6.1)
The moisture content is estimated by sending power through the wood, where the electrical resisôÙ
tance is measured. Lower resistance means higher moisture content and vice versa. The exact
moisture content estimation depends on the wood type and surrounding temperature. TemperaôÙ
ture affects the electrical resistance, where higher temperatures give lower resistance. Details of
moisture content approximation are provided in Appendix F.1.
In addition, the sensor also measures the relative humidity and temperature of the surroundings
since both values influence the moisture content. Relative humidity denotes the ratio of water
content in the air with respect to how much water the air can potentially contain. The air can hold
more water when the temperature increases. The three measurements from Woody can be seen
in Table 6.1.
The sensor is installed by drilling two screws into the wood where power is sent through, as shown
in Figure 6.1. When installed, the customer can choose how often they want the sensor to make
Exploring Machine Learning Methods for Forecasting IoT Measurements in Wooden Structures
67
==PAGE:77==

Moisture content

Table 6.1: Sensor variables measured by Woody sensor.

measurements in either 1, 2, 4, 6, or 12ôÙhour intervals. The most common is one or fourôÙhour
intervals, accounting respectively for 19% and 69% of sensors. The sensors send measurements
to an installed gateway that collects measurements from the sensors and forward the measureôÙ
ments to a database of Woodsense. This enables the customers of Woodsense to monitor the
values of the sensor.
Figure 6.2 shows an example of measurements from a Woody sensor. We can see that the flucôÙ
tuations in moisture and humidity in the first third of the data likely is due to relatively significant
changes in temperature. The slow increase in humidity and moisture is due to a leak that occurred
due to human mistakes which allowed rainwater to seep in. It can also be seen that the humidity
and temperature measurements of these specific sensors follow the outdoor humidity and outdoor
temperature, where moisture content decreases as humidity decreases. This example indicates
that rain, outdoor humidity, and outdoor temperature are useful in forecasting moisture content.
6.1.2
Preprocessing of Sensor Values
This section will describe how the data is preprocessed before being used for training. The proôÙ
cessing is explained in greater detail in Appendix F.2. Sensor measurements were collected from
2020ôÙ06ôÙ08 to 2022ôÙ05ôÙ01.
The preprocessing can be divided into three main steps: ãcleaningã, ãsegmentationã, and ãresamôÙ
pling and interpolating the dataã. ãCleaningã means removing invalid moisture values. ãSegmenôÙ
tationã involves splitting the time series of sensors into multiple segments if there are more than 48
hours between measurements. Thus, segments will be referring to the split time series of a senôÙ
sor. ãResampling and interpolating the dataã involves transforming each segment to have 4ôÙhour
intervals between observations.
Each segment is resampled into fourôÙhour intervals to make the data more structured and easier for
the TFT to learn temporal dependencies. FourôÙhour intervals are chosen since most observations
are with this frequency. If the frequency is below four hours, the observation closest to the time of
the regular intervals is sampled. If the frequency is above fourôÙhour intervals, linear interpolation
is also done to fill missing values. An illustration of resampling and interpolation for data with
frequencies below and above four hours can be seen in Figure 6.3.
Additionally, feature engineering was applied to the timestamps to get timeôÙofôÙday (tod_sin and
tod_cos) and dayôÙofôÙyear (doy_sin and doy_cos) features. The features are extracted the same
way described in Section 5.2.3.
68

==PAGE:78==

fluctuations in the moisture and humidity values which is probably due to variation in temperature.
Afterward, a slow increase in moisture and humidity occurs due to a leak that occured during rain.
Labels on the yôÙaxes mean the following: % MC = % moisture content, % RH = relative humidity

6.1.3
Sensor Tags
Sensor tags involve all static information about the sensor. The sensor tags provide information
regarding the placement and environment of the sensors. The sensor tags depend on customers
giving information which is not always the case. Depending on the tag, there are 7ôÙ25% filled
sensor tags. The only tags that are available for all sensors are ãHardware IDã, ãLongitudeã, and
ãLatitudeã. In Appendix F.3, each type of sensor tag is explained along with percentages of sensors
that have information regarding each tag.

6.1.5
Forecasted Weather Data
As mentioned, daily weather forecasts for 14 days will be used to forecast moisture content. The
API of OpenWeather also retrieves the weather forecasts. Weather forecasts are recorded from
2022ôÙ03ôÙ08. The weather forecasts contain the same features listed in Table 6.2 except it also
has ãprobability of precipitationã. Except for the temperature, there is only one forecast available
per day which is a forecast for noon. The temperature forecasts are provided with minimum,
maximum, night, evening, and morning values.
The daily forecasted weather data needs to be interpolated to fourôÙhour intervals. The temperature
forecasts have exactly six forecasts for each day. It is assumed that the forecasts approximate
the different time intervals of the day, so the forecasts are mapped the following way:
ãÂ Night ã00 : 00
ãÂ Minimum ã04 : 00
ãÂ Morning ã08 : 00
ãÂ Day ã12 : 00
ãÂ Maximum ã16 : 00
ãÂ Evening ã20 : 00
Interpolating the other values is more challenging. Relative humidity depends on temperature,
but it is also dependent on atmospheric pressure2. Therefore, it is not possible to infer the corôÙ
rect relative humidity. It was not possible to find any methods concerning interpolating relative
humidity based on a single humidity observation and multiple temperature observations. Danish
Meteorological Institute was also contacted, but they could not provide any solutions. The decided
method was to use a simple approximation of the relationship between temperature (T), relative
humidity (RH) and dew point (Tdp)3. Dew point denotes the needed temperature for the water in
the air to saturate and has the metric ô¯C.
Tdp ãT ã100 ãRH
5
(6.2)
The dew point is estimated from the ãdayã temperature forecast and humidity forecast. Afterward,
it is assumed that the dew point is constant, and the relative humidity levels at the other intervals
t are then calculated using the forecasted temperatures at time t.
RHt ã100 ã5(Tt ãTdp)
(6.3)
2?iiTb,ff2MXrBFBT2/BXQ`;frBFBf>mKB/Biv
3?iiTb,ff2MXrBFBT2/BXQ`;frBFBf.2rnTQBMi

Exploring Machine Learning Methods for Forecasting IoT Measurements in Wooden Structures
==PAGE:80==
However, the dew point will change during the day, and the estimation is therefore biased. Two
other interpolation techniques were also tested but resulted in higher MAE. The techniques estiôÙ
mate the future relative humidity levels based on the variance of relative humidity over the latest
day. The exact details of the techniques and MAE of each method can be seen in Appendix F.4.
Interpolation of precipitation and snow is even more challenging since interpolation of precipitation
cannot be based on temperature. Therefore, the average distribution of rain and snowfall is calôÙ
culated for each interval in the day based on weather data from the period weather forecasts are
available. The distribution is then multiplied by the accumulated rain during the day. Additionally,
all rain forecasts with a probability below 50% are set to zero.
In Appendix F.4, the distribution of rain and the stepôÙwise interpolation error up to 14 days are
shown.
6.2
Methods
The following section explains the applied methods in developing a model for moisture content
forecasting. Section 6.2.1 explains the procedure for creating a training, validation and test set.
The feature selection method is explained in Section 6.2.2. The training and hyperparameter
optimization settings used throughout development is explained Section 6.2.3.
6.2.1
Training, Validation and Test splits
Each sensor segment is split into training, validation, and test set. Each segment in the validation
and test set contains 168 observations (equivalent to 28 days) such that the first 84 observations
can be used as input for the encoder. There is no overlap between the three sets. During hyôÙ
perparameter optimization, the encoder length varies as it is a hyperparameter. However, it is
ensured that the model always evaluates on the last 84 observations. Thus, if the encoder length
is shorter than 84, then some of the first observations will not be used as input.
The splitting procedure per segment is illustrated on Figure 6.4. If there are below 252 observations
(equivalent to 84 days), then the segment is only split into a training and validation set. If the
segment has below 168 observations, the whole segment is only used in the training set. The
amount of sensors, segments, observations and ratio in % of each set can be seen in Table 6.3.
As mentioned, weather forecasts are recorded from 2022ôÙ03ôÙ08, which means that the model
only can be evaluated on segments that have data from 2022ôÙ03ôÙ08 to 2022ôÙ05ôÙ01. Thus, the
validation and test sets for evaluating the TFT with forecasted weather data as input are subsets
of the whole validation and test sets. Therefore, the evaluation will be biased towards how well
the model performs in March and May 2022. ãRestricted validationã and ãrestricted testã in Table
6.3 refers to these restricted sets.

Figure 6.4: Training, validation and test splits per data segment.
The training splits could also have been done by setting whole sensors aside in the validation
and test sets. However, if the model is applied in production, most of the forecasts will be used
for already installed sensors. It is, therefore, of interest to test how the model performs on new
unseen data of each sensor.

6.2.2
Feature Selection Method
The feature selection method described in this section aims to determine which features improve
forecasting performance. The features are added in groups where the grouped features are kept
in the next iteration if the forecasting error improves.
For each iteration, the model has performed hyperparameter optimization because it is assumed
that the optimal hyperparameters depend on the number of features and the features themselves.
The effect of adding a feature group is only tested once. Therefore, the method is not forward
feature selection. There is not enough time nor computational resources available to make the
proper forward feature selection. It would probably have been feasible if a fixed set of hyperparamôÙ
eters were chosen. The choice was, therefore, between hyperparameter tuning or conventional
forward feature selection. It was assumed there is a greater risk associated with getting a bad set
of hyperparameters due to fixed hyperparameters are randomly suited for the set of features.
The chosen method, of course, has the disadvantage that it is not possible to infer the importance
of a single feature, and an unimportant feature can be added because it is part of a group. Also,
the testing of adding the feature can lead to the risk of missing features, which will only improve the
model if other features are present. To mitigate the risk of choosing unimportant features or missing
features, each grouping of features is grouped based on the features either containing similar
information or their information complements each other. The model with the lowest validation
loss is retrained five times to estimate training uncertainty because performance is found to vary
significantly per training run. This mitigates the risk that features are chosen due to a good random
initialization of weight parameters.
Baseline: The baseline model has moisture, temperature, and humidity as observed inputs to
the encoder only and tod_sin, tod_cos, doy_sin, and doy_cos as known inputs that are fed into
both the encoder and decoder. The model does not use any static covariates. The model mainly
functions as a baseline to test if the weather features give better forecasting performance. The
model is named ãBaselineã.
Weather Features: This model will have the same features as the baseline model, but with outôÙ
door relative humidity, outdoor temperature, precipitation, and snowfall added. Wind speed and
direction are excluded from this group of features. They will be added later because information
first is available from 2021ôÙ07ôÙ22, which creates some challenges in handling missing features,
which are explored on their own, as can be read below. The model is named ãWeatherã.
Sensor tags as static covariates: The sensor tags ãprimary element categoryã, ãclimateã, ãventiôÙ
lationã, ãinsulationã, and ãscrewôÙtypeã tags are added as static covariates to test if they can improve
the model by providing contextual environmental information such as how exposed they are to outôÙ
side weather. These tags are chosen because they have the highest percentage of information
for the sensors. The model is named ãStatic tagsã.
Target mean and variance static covariates: Even though the model input is globally standardôÙ
ized, it is possible that the information concerning the scale of inputs can be lost in the many
nonôÙlinear layers. Therefore, it is tested if using the mean and variance of the target series enôÙ
coder input as static covariates can help the model handle scale better. The model is named
ãTarget scalesã.
Hardware ID: Adding Hardware ID as a static covariate can enable the model to fit the behavior of
72
Exploring Machine Learning Methods for Forecasting IoT Measurements in Wooden Structures
==PAGE:82==
the single sensor better. However, adding the feature also introduces some challenges. Firstly, the
TFT must be fitted to each sensor before it can make forecasts. This is not practical because this
would mean the model needs to be trained anew every time there is a new sensor. This challenge
is being handled by setting the Hardware ID embedding vector to zero for random training samples
in a miniôÙbatch. This way, the model should learn to handle sensors it has not seen before.
This introduces a challenge in determining the probability of zeroing the embedding vector, which
could be another hyperparameter. However, the optimal hyperparameter will likely be 0% probaôÙ
bility for zeroing the embedding vector. Instead, the probability is set to the average percentage of
new sensors introduced each month which is 14.5%. Thus, assuming the model will be retrained
every month, then 14.5% of its forecasts will be for new sensors during that month. This way, the
model will be trained on a scenario equivalent to how the model will be used. The model is named
ãHardware IDã.
Equilibrium Moisture Content: The feature Equilibrium Moisture Content (EMC) as described in
Section 1.2 denotes the expected moisture content assuming the current humidity and temperature
are constant in infinite time. The feature can potentially give the model information in what direction
the moisture content is moving. The EMC is added as an observed input to the encoder. It can
potentially lead to a more challenging interpretation since EMC is calculated from humidity and
temperature. The model is named ãEMCã.
Wind direction and wind speed: The wind direction and wind speed features might result in
better forecasting of moisture content during rain since the direction of the wind can determine if
the rain hits the wood element. The wind directions are mapped from degrees to the united circle
resulting in two features named ãwind_dir_cosã and ãwind_dir_sinã. Wind direction and wind speed
data are not available for 72% of all training time series which creates a challenge in handling
the missing values. It was only possible to find literature handling missing values by imputation
techniques for data that is missing randomly [94, 95, 96]. Thus, these imputation methods are
unlikely to work well as wind data is missing in whole segments. Therefore, there is experimented
with two methods to enable the model to handle missing values by substituting the missing values
with constants to make the model learn the data is missing. The two methods are named ãWind
1ã and ãWind 2ã, respectively.
Method one substitutes missing values in ãwind_dir_cosã and ãwind_dir_sinã with zero, which will
be equivalent to the center of the unit circle. Wind speed is set to ôÙ1 when the data is missing
because a wind speed of ôÙ1 is not a possible value. By filling missing values with 0 and ôÙ1, it is
assumed that the model should be able to interpret these values as missing and adjust accordôÙ
ingly. The second method also has the value imputations as above but has two additional boolean
features denoting if the data is available.
6.2.3
Training and Hyperparameter Optimization Settings
This section will describe the training and hyperparameter settings shared by all models.
All feature selection models during hyperparameter tuning use an early stopping with ãpatience of
10 and ãminimum improvementã of 0.001. The validation loss is evaluated five times per epoch.
Practically, this means the model is stopped if the validation loss does not improve for two epochs.
Otherwise, the model will train for ten epochs. The model parameters obtaining the lowest valiôÙ
dation loss are saved automatically to get the optimal model. All models also use learning rate
decay, where the learning rate is halved if the validation loss is not lower than the validation loss
of the previous epoch. After hyperparameter optimization was performed for feature selection, it
was concluded that the ãpatienceã set to 10 resulted in training stopped prematurely as training
loss still was improving for some models (Figure F.8 in Appendix F.6). Therefore, it was adjusted
to 20 during the five retrainings for calculating mean validation losses during feature comparisons.
All realôÙvalued inputs are standardized globally such that the model can interpret the scale of the
current inputs. There were experimented with three standardization methods of the target series as
the global standardization used in prior experiments led to bad results. The three methods all stanôÙ
dardize the target series (i.e., moisture observations) but differ in how ôç and ü are estimated. The
global method estimates the parameters based on the whole training set. The grouping method
estimates the parameters from the time series segment in the training set the target series belongs
Exploring Machine Learning Methods for Forecasting IoT Measurements in Wooden Structures
73
==PAGE:83==
to. The individual method estimates the parameters from the encoder input from which the target
series is forecasted. The individual standardization method was found to improve forecasting sigôÙ
nificantly. In Appendix F.5, the three standardization methods are explained in more detail, and
the MAE and RMSE validation errors for each standardization method are shown for the optimal
model after feature selection.
A possible explanation for individual standardization works best is that the scale of outputs of
neural networks is not sensitive to the scale of inputs due to the many nonôÙlinear transformations
done in the network [28]. The scale and variance of moisture values are relatively different, where
some have low and high variance and have an average moisture content between 5ôÙ25% MC.
By standardizing each target series, the model will automatically always create forecasts close to
zero, which makes training easier. Also, by making individual scaling of each feature, the model
is trained to predict relative changes, which is precisely what is of interest.
Hyperparameter optimization is done with 40 samples. All models in feature selection have the
same hyperparameter range, which is documented in Appendix F.6. However, it was concluded
that the ãencoder lengthã and ãnumber of attention headsã ranges were too narrow during feature
selection. Therefore, a final hyperparameter optimization was conducted with broader ranges in
ãencoder lengthã and ãnumber of attention headsã on the model with the best set of features. The
patience was also adjusted from 10 to 20. Additionally, the number of trials was raised to 60 to
encompass the broader intervals. The hyperparameter range and optimal hyperparameters for
the final model is documented in Appendix F.7.
6.2.4
Evaluation Metrics
The following section will explain the applied metrics to evaluate forecasting performance. As in the
former section, probabilistic forecasts are evaluated with 0.1 and 0.9 quantile loss which measures
how well the model respectively provides lower and upper prediction intervals. RMSE and MAE are
also applied to evaluate the median forecast. MAE will give an estimate of the average absolute
error. This is useful in learning what errors that generally can be expected. However, there are
many segments with more or less constant moisture values. Therefore, RMSE is also applied
as it will give a better indication of how the model performs on samples where moisture content
changes as higher errors are penalized more.

with the best set of features.

Discussion of Validation Results
It is evaluated that the model still obtains useable forecasts when applied with weather forecasts
as it still gets lower errors than the naive forecaster on almost all steps. It was feared that the
model would perform dramatically differently when applied with the interpolated weather forecasts
as the interpolations only mimic how the features behave during the day. However, it does not
seem to be the case. Therefore, it is decided not to make any adjustments before training the
model on the combined training and validation set.

between the final model with historic weather forecasts and the naive baseline on the restricted
test set is not as significant as in the restricted validation set shown in Figure 6.8. The naive
baseline is better than the forecasts with historic weather data at some horizons.
The difference between forecasts with historic and forecasted weather data is significantly more
different than the stepôÙwise errors on the restricted validation set. The forecast errors with weather
forecasts as input are spiking around specific forecasting horizons, which is most evident around
forecasting horizon 60.
